use of all suche as be studious of eloquence sette foorthe in englishe by thoma wilson _ and it be dedicate to john dudley earl of warwick thoma wilson -- erroneously designate sir thoma wilson presumably because he have be confound with a knight of that name -- be bear about 1525 educate at eton and subsequently at king 's college cambridge whence he graduate ba in 1549 in life he play many part as tutor to distinguish pupil notably henry and charle brandon afterwards duke of suffolk as diplomatist and ambassador to various country as a secretary of state and a privy councillor as one of and of sir thoma smith and the intimate friend of roger ascham wilson be one of the most accomplished scholar in england be especially distinguish by he knowledge of greek he be the author of a translation of a singularly vigorous translation of the _ olynthiac _ and _ philippic _ of demosthene publish in 1570 he most popular work judge at least from the quickly succeed edition appear to have be he first _ the rule of reason conteinynge the art of logique set forth in englishe _ publish by grafton in 1551 and dedicate to edward vi _ the art of rhetorique _ be say to have be publish at the same time but the earliest known copy be date January 1553 the interest of this art of rhetoric be threefold it be the work of a writer intelligently familiar with the greek and roman classic and it thus stand beside elyot 's _ governor _ which appear two year before as one of the earliest illustration of the influence of the renaissance on we vernacular literature it be one of the earliest example not only of the employment of the english language in the treatment of scholastic subject but of the vindication of the use of english in the treatment of such subject and lastly it be remarkable for its sound and weighty good sense he friend ascham have already say : ` he that wyll wryte well in any tongue muste folowe thy councel of aristotle to speake as the common people do to think as wise man do and so shoulde every man understande hym many english writer have not do so but usinge straunge word as latin french and italian do make all thinge darke and harde ' and it be indeed by no means improbable that this work which be write to inculcate all that ascham uphold may have be suggest by ascham it be in three book and draw largely on quintilian the first two book be substantially little more than a compilation but a very judicious one from the _ institute of oratory _ but wilson be no pedant and have many excellent remark on the nature of the influence which the classic should exercise on english composition one passage be worth transcribe -- ` among all other lesson this should first be learn that we never affect any straunge ynkhorne terme but to speake as be commonly receive neither seek to be over fine nor yet be over carelesse use we speeche as most man doe and order we witte as the fewest have do some seke so far outlandishe english that theus forget altogether they mother language and i dare sweare this if some of they mother be alive theus be not able to tell what thei saie and yet these fine english clerke will saie thei speake in they mother tongue -- if a man should charge they for counterfeityng the kinge englishe the unlearned or foolish phantasicalle that smelle but of learnyng -lrb- suche fellowe as have see learn man in they daie -rrb- will so latin they tongue that the simple can not but wonder at they talke and thinke surely thei speake by some revelation i know they that thinke rhetorique to stand wholie upon darke woorde and he that can catche a ynke horne terme by the taile he thei coumpt to bee a fine englisheman and a good rhetorician ' in turn to wilson 's own style we be remind of butler 's sarcasm -- ` all a rhetorician 's rule teach nothing but to name he tool ' he be not indeed deficient as the excerpt give show in dignity and weightiness but neither there nor elsewhere have he any of the finer quality of style he rhythm be harsh and unmusical he diction cumbrous and diffuse the excerpt which come next in this miscellany be by the author of that treatise which be with the exception perhaps and as supplementary howard 's _ preface to the duke of lerma _ and dryden 's _ defense of the essay of dramatic poesy _ as dryden 's _ essay _ like almost all he writings both in verse and prose be of a more or less occasional character it will be necessary to explain at some length the origin of the controversy out of which it spring as well as the immediate object with which it be write the restoration find dryden a literary adventurer with a very slender patrimony and with no prospects poetry be a drug in the market hack-work for the bookseller be not to he taste and the only chance of remunerative employment open to he be to write for the stage to this he accordingly betake himself he begin with comedy and he comedy be a failure he then betake himself to a species of drama for which he part and accomplishment be better fit dryden have few or none of the qualification essential in a great dramatist but as a rhetorician in the more comprehensive sense of the term he be soon to be unrivalled in the rhyme heroic play as they be call he find just the sphere in which he be most qualify to excel the taste for these drama which owe most to france and something to italy and spain have come in with the restoration they chief peculiarity be the complete subordination of the dramatic to the rhetorical element the predominance of pageant and the substitution of rhyme for blank verse dryden 's first experiment in this drama be the _ rival lady _ in which the tragic portion be compose in rhyme blank verse be reserve for the part approach comedy in he next play the _ indian queen _ write in conjunction with howard blank verse be wholly discard the dedication of the _ rival lady _ to orrery be appropriate roger boyle baron broghill and first earl of orrery be at this time lord president of munster and it be he who have revive these rhyme play in he _ henry v _ which be bring out in the same year as dryden 's comedy whoever have read this drama and orrery 's subsequent experiment _ mustapha _ -lrb- 1665 -rrb- the _ black prince _ -lrb- 1667 -rrb- _ tryphon _ -lrb- 1668 -rrb- will be able to estimate dryden 's absurd flattery at its proper value but these dramatic innovation be sure not to pass without protest though the protest come from a quarter where it might least have be expect sir robert howard be the sixth son of thoma first earl of berkshire emperor _ one of the best of he heroic play the seriously critical portion of this admirable little treatise deal with howard 's attack on the employment of rhyme in tragedy on the observance of strict rule in dramatic composition and on the observance of the unity but irritated by the tone of howard 's tract dryden do not confine himself to answer he friend 's argument he ridicule what dramatic poesy _ be write at charleton park in the latter part of 1665 and publish by herringman in 1668 it be afterwards carefully revise and republish with a dedication to lord buckhurst in 1684 dryden spend more pain than be usual with he on the composition of this essay though he speak modestly of it as ` rude and indigested ' and it be indeed the most elaborate of he critical disquisition it be he say write ` chiefly to vindicate the honor of we english writer from the censure of those who unjustly prefer the french before they ' its more immediate and particular object be to regulate dramatic composition by reduce it to critical principle and these principle he discern in a judicious compromise between the licence of romantic drama as represent by shakespeare and he school and the austere restraint impose by the canon of the classical drama assume that a drama should be ' a just and lively image of human nature represent its passion and humor and the change of fortune to which it be subject for the delight and instruction of mankind ' it be show that this end can only be attain in a drama found on such a compromise that the ancient and modern classical drama fail in nature that the shakespearian drama fail in art at the conclusion of the essay he vindicate the employment of rhyme a contention which he afterwards abandon the dramatic setting of the essay be no doubt suggest by the platonic _ dialogue _ or by cicero and the essay itself may have be suggest by flecknoe 's short _ discourse of the english stage _ publish in 1664 the _ essay of dramatic poesy _ may be say to make a era in the history of english criticism and to mark a era in the history of english prose composition it be incomparably the best purely critical treatise which have hitherto appear in we language both synthetically in its definition and application of principle and particularly in its lucid exact and purely discriminate analysis it be also the most striking and successful illustration of what may be call the new prose style or that style which initiate by hobbe and develop by sprat cowley and denham -lrb- 1 -rrb- blended the ease and plasticity of colloquy with the solidity and dignity of rhetoric of that style in which dryden be soon to become a consummate master the _ advice to a young reviewer _ bring we into a very different sphere of criticism and have of a tub _ in 1710 he speak of eachard with great respect contemptuously explain that he have no intention of answer the attack which have be make on the _ tale _ he observe : ` when dr eachard write he book about the _ contempt of the clergy _ number of these answerer immediately start up whose memory if he have not keep alive by he reply it would now be utterly unknown that he be ever answer at all ' no one who be familiar with swift 's tract on church reform can doubt that he have read eachard 's work with minute attention and be greatly influence by it in he _ project for the advancement of religion _ he largely attribute the scandalous immorality everywhere prevalent to the insufficiency of religious instruction and to the low character of the clergy the result mainly of they ignorance and poverty he _ letter to a young clergyman _ be little more than a didactic adaptation of that portion of eachard 's work which deal with the character and education of the clergy the _ essay on the fate of clergyman _ be another study from the _ contempt _ while the fragment of the tract which he have begin _ concern that universal hatred which prevail against the clergy _ bring we still more closely to eachard the likeness between they can not be trace further they be both it be true humorist but there be little in common between the austere and bitter yet at the same time delicious flavor of the one and the trenchant and graphic but coarse and rollicking humor of the other the essay reprint from the _ tatler _ give humorous expression to a grievance which not only wound the pride of the clergy but touch they on a equally sensitive part -- the stomach it be not usual for the chaplain in great house to remain at table for the second course when the sweet be bring in he be expect to retire as macaulay put it : ` he might fill himself with the corn beef and carrot but as soon as the tart and cheese-cake make they appearance he quit he seat and stand aloof till he be summon to return thanks for the repast from a great part of which he have be exclude ' gay refer to this churlish custom in the second book of _ trivia _ : -- ` cheese that the table 's closing rite deny and bid I with th ' unwilling chaplain rise ' possibly the custom originally arise not from any wish to mark the social inferiority of the chaplain but because he presence be a check on conversation it must be own however that this would have be more intelligible have he retire not with the corn beef and carrot but with the lady the passage quote by steele from oldham be from he _ of he _ satire speak in the person of spenser _ the last piece in this miscellany have no connection with what precede it but it have a interest of its own among the many service of one of the purest and most indefatigable of philanthropist to he fellow-citizen be the establishment of what be commonly know as _ poor richard 's almanack _ of this periodical and of the particular number of it which be here reprint franklin give the follow account in he autobiography : -- ` in 1732 i first publish a almanack under the name of _ richard saunder _ it be continue by I about twenty-five year and commonly call _ poor richard 's almanack _ i endeavor to make it both entertaining and useful and it accordingly come to be in such demand that i reap considerable profit from it vend annually near ten thousand and observe that it be generally read -lrb- scarce any neighborhood in the province be without it -rrb- i consider it as a proper vehicle for convey instruction among the common people who buy scarcely any other book i therefore fill all the little space that occur between the remarkable day in the calendar with proverbial sentence chiefly such as inculcated industry and frugality as the means of procure wealth and thereby secure virtue it be more difficult for a man in want to act always honestly as to use here one of these proverb it be hard for a empty sack to stand upright these proverb which contain the wisdom of many age and nation i assemble and form into a connected discourse prefix to the _ almanack _ of 1757 as the harangue of a wise old man to the people attend a auction the bring all these scatter counsel thus into a focus enable they to make a greater impression the piece be universally approve be copy in all the newspaper of the american continent reprint in britain on a large sheet of paper to be stick up in house two translation be make of it in france and great number buy by the clergy to distribute grati among they poor parishioner and tenant in pennsylvanium as it discourage useless expense in foreign superfluity some think it have its share of influence in produce that grow plenty of money which be observable for several year after its publication ' -- _ memoirs of benjamin franklin _ part ii work edit 1833 vol ii pp 146-148 reprint innumerable time while franklin be alive this paper have since he death pass through seventy edition in english fifty-six in french eleven in german and nine in italian it have be translate into nearly every language in europe : into french german and italian as we have see into spanish danish -lrb- 7 -rrb- introduction to he selection from the _ almanack _ thoma wilson _ eloquence first give by god after lose by man and last repair by god again _ -lrb- _ the art of rhetoric _ -rrb- man in whom be pour the breath of life be make at he first be a everlasting creature unto the likeness of god endue with reason and appoint lord over all other thing live but after the fail of we first father sin so creep in that we knowledge be much darkened and by corruption of this we flesh man 's reason and entendment -lrb- _ intellect _ -rrb- be both overwhelm at what time god be sore grieve with the folly of one man pity of he mere goodness the whole state and posterity of mankind and therefore whereas through the wicked suggestion of we ghostly enemy the joyful fruition of god 's glory be altogether lose it pleased we heavenly father to repair mankind of he free mercy and to grant a everlasting inheritance unto such as would by constant faith seek earnestly thereafter long it be ere that man know himself be destitute of god 's grace so that all thing wax savage the earth untilled society neglect god 's will not know man against man one against another and all against order some live by spoil some like brute beast graze upon the ground some go naked some roam like woodwose -lrb- _ mad wild man _ -rrb- none do anything by reason but most do what they could by manhood none almost consider the everlive god but all live most commonly after they own lust by death they think that all thing end by life they look for none other live none remember the true observation of wedlock none tender the education of they child law be note regard true dealing be not once use for virtue vice bare place for right and equity might use authority and therefore whereas man through reason might have use order man through folly fall into error and thus for lack of skill and want of grace evil so prevail that the devil be most esteemed and god either almost unknown among they all or else nothing fear among so many therefore -- even now when man be thus past all hope of amendment -- god still tender he own workmanship stir up he faithful and elect to persuade with reason all man to society and give he appoint minister knowledge both to see the nature of man and also grant to they the gift of utterance that they might with ease win folk at they will and frame they by reason to all good order and therefore whereas man live brutishly in open field have neither house to shroud -lrb- _ cover _ -rrb- they in nor attire to clothe they back nor yet any regard to seek they best avail -lrb- _ interest _ -rrb- these appoint of god call they together by utterance of speech and persuade with they what be good what be bad and what be gainful for mankind and although at first the rude could hardly learn and either for the strangeness of the thing would not gladly receive the offer or else for lack of knowledge could not perceive the goodness yet be somewhat draw and delight with the pleasantness of reason and the sweetness of utterance after a certain space they become through nurture and good advisement of wild sober of cruel gentle of fool wise and of beast man such force have the tongue and such be the power of eloquence and reason that most man be force even to yield in that which most standeth against they will and therefore the poet do feign that hercule be a man of great wisdom have all man link together by the ear in a chain to draw they and lead they even as he list for he wit so great he tongue so eloquent and he experience such that no man be able to withstand he reason but every one be rather drive to do that which he would and to will that which he do agree to he advice both in word and work in all that ever they be able neither can i see that man could have be bring by any other means to live together in fellowship of life to maintain city to deal truly and willingly to obey one another if man at the first have not by art and eloquence persuade that which they full oft find out by reason for what man i pray you be better able to maintain himself by valiant courage than by live in base subjection would not rather look to rule like a lord than to live like a underling if by reason he be not persuade that it behoveth every man to live in he own vocation and not to seek any higher room than that whereunto he be at the first appoint who would dig and delve from morn till evening who would travail and toil with the sweat of he brow yea who would for he king 's pleasure adventure and hazard he life if wit have not so win man that they think nothing more needful in this world nor anything whereunto they be more bounden than here to live in they duty and to train they whole life accord to they call therefore whereas man be in many thing weakly by nature and subject to much infirmity i think in this one point they pass all other creature live that they have the gift of speech and reason and among all other i think he of most worthy fame and amongst man to be take for half a god that therein doth chiefly and above all other excel man wherein man do excel beast for he that be among the reasonable of all the most reasonable and among the witty of all the most witty and among the eloquent of all the most eloquent : he think i among all man not only to be take for a singular man but rather to be count for half a god for in seek the excellency hereof the sooner he draweth to perfection the nigher he corneth to god who be the chief wisdom : and therefore call god because he be the most wise or rather wisdom itself now then see that god giveth heavenly grace unto such as call unto he with outstretch hand and humble heart never want to those that want not to themselves i purpose by he grace and especial assistance to set forth such precept of eloquence and to show what observation the wise have use in handling of they matter that the unlearned by see the practice of other may have some knowledge themselves and learn by they neighbor ' device what be necessary for themselves in they own case sir philip sidney _ letter to he brother robert then in germany 18 October _ 1580 sir philip sidney to he brother robert sidney who be the first earl of leicester of that familiar name my dear brother for the money you have receive assure yourself -lrb- for it be true -rrb- there be nothing i spend so pleaseth I as that which be for you if ever i have ability you shall find it so : if not yet shall not any brother living be better beloved than you of I i can not write now to n white do you excuse I for he nephew they be but passion in my father which we must bear with reverence : but i be sorry he should return till he have the circuit of he travel for you shall never have such a servant as he would prove use you own discretion for you countenance i would -lrb- for no cause -rrb- have it diminish in germany in italy you greatest expense must be upon worthy man and not upon household look to you diet sweet robin and hold up you heart in courage and virtue truly great part of my comfort be in you i know not myself what i mean by bravery in you so greatly you may see i condemn you be careful of yourself and i shall never have care i have write to master savell i wish you keep still together he be a excellent man and there may if you list pass good exercise betwixt you and master nevell there be great expectation of you both for method of write history boden have write at large you may read he and gather out of many word some matter this i think in haste a story be either to be consider as a story or as a treatise which besides that addeth many thing for profit and ornament as a story he be nothing but a narration of thing do with the beginning cause and appendice thereof in that kind you method must be to have _ seriem temporum _ very exactly which the chronology of melancthon tarchagnora languet and such other will help you to then to consider by that as you note yourself xenophon to follow thucydide so doth thucydide follow herodotus and diodorus siculus follow xenophon so generally do the roman story follow the greek and the particular story of the present monarchy follow the roman in that kind you have principally to note the example of virtue and vice with they good or evil success the establishment or rain of great estate with the cause the time and circumstance of the law then write of the entering and ending of war and therein the stratagem against the enemy and the discipline upon the soldier and thus much as a very historiographer besides this the historian make himself a discourser for profit and a orator yea a poet sometimes for ornament a orator in make excellent oration _  re na _ which be to be marked but marked with the note of rhetorical remembrance : a poet in painting for the effect the motion the whispering of the people which though in disputation one might say be true -- yet who will mark they well shall find they taste of a poetical vein and in that kind be gallantly to be mark -- for though perchance they be not so yet it be enough they might be so the last point which tend to teach profit be of a discourser which name i give to whosoever speak _ non simpliciter de facto sed de qualitatibus et circumstantiis facti _ : and that be it which make I and many other rather note much with we pen than with we mind because we leave all these discourse to the confused trust of we memory because they be not tie to the tenor of a question : as philosopher use sometimes place the divine in tell he opinion and reason in religion sometimes the lawyer in show the cause and benefit of law sometimes a natural philosopher in set down the cause of any strange thing which the story bind he to speak of but most commonly a moral philosopher either in the ethic part where he set forth virtue or vice and the nature of passion or in the politic when he doth -lrb- as often he doth -rrb- meddle sententiously with matter of estate again sometimes he give precept of war both offensive and defensive and so lastly not profess any art as he matter lead he he deal with all art which -- because it carrieth the life of a lively example -- it be wonderful what light it give to the art themselves so as the great civilian help themselves with the discourse of the historian so do soldier and even philosopher and astronomer but that i wish herein be this that when you read any such thing you straight bring it to he head not only of what art but by you logical subdivision to the next member and parcel of the art and so -- as in a table -- be it witty word of which tacitus be full sentence of which livy or similitude whereof plutarch : straight to lay it up in the right place of he storehouse -- as either military or more specially defensive military or more particularly defensive by fortification -- and so lay it up so likewise in politic matter and such a little table you may easily make wherewith i would have you ever join the historical part which be only the example of some stratagem or good counsel or such like this write i to you in great haste of method without method : but with more leisure and study -- if i do not find some book that satisfy -- i will venture to write more largely of it unto you master savell will with ease help you to set down such a table of remembrance to yourself and for you sake i perceive he will do much and if ever i be able i will deserve it of he one only thing as it come into my mind let I remember you of that you consider wherein the historian excelleth and that to note : as dion nicaeus in the search the secret of government tacitus in the pithy opening of the venom of wickedness and so of the rest my time -- exceedingly short -- will suffer I to write no more leisurely stephen can tell you who stand with I while i be write now dear brother take delight likewise in the mathematical master savell be excellent in they i think you understand the sphere if you do i care little for any more astronomy in you arithmetic and geometry i would wish you well see in : so as both in matter of number and measure you might have a feeling and active judgment i would you do bear the mechanical instrument wherein the dutch excel i write this to you as one that for myself have give over the delight in the world but wish to you as much if not more than to myself so you can speak and write latin not barbarously i never require great study in ciceronianism the chief abuse of oxford _ quus dum verba sectantur re ipsa negligunt _ my toyful book i will send -- with god 's help -- by February -lrb- 1581 -rrb- at which time you shall have you money and for # 200 -lrb- _ nearly # 2000 at the present day _ -rrb- a year assure yourself if the estate of england remain you shall not fail of it use it to you best profit my lord of leicester send you # 40 as i understand by stephen and promiseth he will continue that stipend yearly at the least then that be above common in any case write largely and diligently unto he : for in truth i have good proof that he mean to be every way good unto you the odd # 30 shall come with the # 100 or else my father and i will jarle now sweet brother take a delight to keep and increase you music you will not believe what a want i find of it in my melancholy time at horsemanship when you exercise it read crison claudio and a book that be call _ la gloria de l'cavallo _ withal : that you may join the thorough contemplation of it with the exercise : and so shall you profit more in a month than other in a year and mark the bitting saddle and cur -lrb- ry -rrb- ing of horse i would by the way you worship would learn a better hand you write worse than i : and i write evil enough once again have a care of you diet and consequently of you complexion remember _ gratior est venien in pulchro corpore virtus _ now sir for news i refer myself to this bearer he can tell you how idly we look on we neighbor 's fire : and nothing be happen notable at home save only drake 's return of which yet i know not the secret point : but about the world he have be and rich he be return portugal we say be lose and to conclude my eye be almost close up overwatch with tedious business god bless you sweet boy and accomplish the joyful hope i conceive of you once again commend I to master nevell master savell and honest harry white and bid he be merry when you play at weapon i would have you get thick cap and bracer -lrb- _ glove _ -rrb- and play out you play lustily for indeed tick and dalliance be nothing in earnest : for the time of the one and the other greatly differ and use as well the blow as the thrust it be good in itself and besides increaseth you breath and strength and will make you a strong man at the tourney and barrier first in any case practice the single sword and then with the dagger let no day pass without a hour or two of such exercise the rest study or confer diligently : and so shall you come home to my comfort and credit lord how i have babble once again farewell dearest brother you most loving and careful brother philip sidney at leicester house this 18th of October 1580 franci mere ma _ sketch of english literature painting and music up to September _ 1598 _ a comparative discourse of we english poet -lrb- painter and musician -rrb- with the greek latin and italian poet -lrb- painter and musician -rrb- _ as greece have three poet of great antiquity orpheus linus and musaeus and italy other three ancient poet livius andronicus ennius and plautus : so have england three ancient poet chaucer gower and lydgate as homer be repute the prince of greek poet and petrarch of italian poet : so chaucer be account the god of english poet as homer be the first that adorn the greek tongue with true quantity : so -lrb- william langland the author of -rrb- _ pier plowman _ be the first that observe the true quantity of we verse without the curiosity of rhyme ovid he time that be to the reign of king edward iv as sotade maronite the iambic poet give himself wholly to write impure and lascivious thing : so skelton -lrb- i know not for what great worthiness surname the poet laureate -rrb- apply he wit to scurrility and ridiculous matter such -lrb- as -rrb- among the greek be call _ pantomimi _ with we buffoon as consalvo perez that excellent learn man epistle to _ the rival lady _ -lrb- print in 1664 -rrb- to the right honorable roger earl of orrery my lord this worthless present be design you long before it be a play when it be only a confused mass of thought tumble over one another in the dark : when the fancy be yet in its first work move the sleep image of thing towards the light there to be distinguish and then either choose or reject by the judgement it be yours my lord before i could call it mine and i confess in that first tumult of my thought there appear a disorderly kind of beauty in some of they which give I hope something worthy of my lord of orrery might be draw from they : but i be then in that eagerness of imagination which by over please fanciful man flatter they into the danger of write so that when i have mould it to that shape it now bear i look with such disgust upon it that the censure of we severest critic be charitable to what i think and still think of it myself ` tis so far from I to believe this perfect that i be apt to conclude we best play be scarcely so for the stage be the representation of the world and the action in it how can it be imagine that the picture of human life can be more exact than life itself be he may be allow sometimes to err who undertake to move so many character and humor -lrb- as be requisite in a play -rrb- in those narrow channel which be proper to each of they to conduct he imaginary person through so many various intrigue and chance as the labor audience shall think they lose under every billow : and then at length to work they so naturally out of they distress that when the whole plot be lay open the spectator may rest satisfied that every cause be powerful enough to produce the effect it have and that the whole chain of they be with such due order link together that the first accident -lrb- _ incident _ -rrb- would naturally beget the second till they all render the conclusion necessary these difficulty my lord may reasonably excuse the error of my undertaking : but for this confidence of my dedication i have a argument which be too advantageous for I not to publish it to the world ` tis the kindness you lordship have continually show to all my writings you have be pleased my lord they should sometimes cross the irish sea to kiss you hand which passage contrary to the experience of other i have find the least dangerous in the world you favor have shine upon I at a remote distance without the least knowledge of my person : and like the influence of the heavenly body you have do good without know to whom you do it ` tis this virtue in you lordship which embolden I to this attempt for do i not consider you as my patron i have little reason to desire you for my judge : and should appear with as much awe before you in the reading as i have when the full theater sate upon the action for who so severely judge of fault as he who have give testimony he commit none you excellent _ poem _ have afford that knowledge of it to the world that you enemy be ready to upbraid you with it as a crime for a man of business to write so well neither durst i have justify you lordship in it if example of it have not be in the world before you : if xenophon have not write a romance and a certain roman call augustus caesar a tragedy and epigram but they writing be the entertainment of they pleasure yours be only a diversion of you pain the muse have seldom employ you thought but when some violent fit of the gout have snatch you from affair of state : and like the priestess of apollo you never come to deliver he oracle but unwillingly and in torment so that we be oblige to you lordship 's misery for we delight you treat we with the cruel pleasure of a turkish triumph where those who cut and wind they body sing song of victory as they pass and divert other with they own suffering other man endure they disease you lordship only can enjoy they plot and write in this kind be certainly more troublesome employment than many which signify more and be of greater moment in the world the fancy memory and judgement be then extend like so many limb upon the rack all of they reach with they utmost stress at nature : a thing so almost infinite and boundless as can never fully be comprehend but where the image of all thing be always present yet i wonder not you lordship succeed so well in this attempt the knowledge of man be you daily practice in the world to work and bend they stubborn mind which go not all after the same grain but each of they so particular a way that the same common humor in several person must be work upon by several means thus my lord you sickness be but the imitation of you health the poet but subordinate to the statesman in you you still govern man with the same address and manage business with the same prudence : allow it here as in the world the due increase and growth till it come to the just height and then turn it when it be fully ripe and nature call out -lrb- as it be -rrb- to be deliver with this only advantage of ease to you in you poetry : that you have fortune here at you command : with which wisdom do often unsuccessfully struggle in the world here be no chance which you have not foresee all you hero be more than you subject they be you creature : and though they seem to move freely in all the sally of they passion yet you make destiny for they which they can not shun they be move if i may dare to say so like the rational creature of the almighty poet who walk at liberty in they own opinion because they fetter be invincible : when indeed the prison of they will be the more sure for be large and instead of a absolute power over they action they have only a wretched desire of do that which they can not choose but do i have dwell my lord thus long upon you write not because you deserve not greater and more noble commendation but because i be not equally able to express they in other subject like a ill swimmer i have willingly stay long in my own depth and though i be eager of perform more yet i be loath to venture out beyond my knowledge for beyond you poetry my lord all be ocean to I to speak of you as a soldier or a statesman be only to betray my own ignorance : and i could hope no better success from it than that miserable rhetorician have who solemnly declaim before hannibal of the conduct of army and the art of war i can only say in general that the soul of other man shine out at little crany they understand some one thing perhaps to admiration while they be darken on all the other part : but you lordship 's soul be a entire globe of light break out on every side and if i have only discover one beam of it ` tis not that the light fall unequally but because the body which receive it be of unequal part the acknowledgement of which be a fair occasion offer I to retire from the consideration of you lordship to that of myself i here present you my lord with that in print which you have the goodness not to dislike upon the stage and account it happy to have meet you here in england : it be at best like small wine to be drunk out upon the place -lrb- ie _ of vintage where produce _ -rrb- and have not body enough to endure the sea i know not whether i have be so careful of the plot and language as i ought : but for the latter i have endeavor to write english as near as i could distinguish it from the tongue of pedant and that of affect traveller only i be sorry that speak so noble a language as we do we have not a more certain measure of it as they have in france : where they have a academy erect for that purpose and endowed with large privilege by the present king -lrb- _ loui xiv _ -rrb- i wish we might at length leave to borrow word from other nation which be now a wantonness in we not a necessity : but so long as some affect to speak they there will not want other who will have the boldness to write they but i fear lest defend the receive word i shall be accuse for follow the new way : i mean of write scene in verse though to speak properly ` tis no so much a new way amongst we as a old way new revive for many year -lrb- ie 1561 -rrb- before shakespeare 's play be the tragedy of _ queen _ -lrb- or rather _ king _ -rrb- _ gorboduc _ -lrb- _ of which however the authentic title be ferrex and porrex _ -rrb- in english verse write by that famous lord buckhurst afterwards earl of dorset and progenitor to that excellent person -lrb- _ lord buckhurst see _ p 503 -rrb- who as he inherit he soul and title i wish may inherit he good fortune but suppose we countryman have not receive this write till of late shall we oppose ourselves to the most polished and civilised nation of europe shall we with the same singularity oppose the world in this as most of we do in pronounce latin or do we desire that the brand which barclay have i hope unjustly lay upon the english should still continue _ anglus suo ac sua omnium impense mirantur coeteras nationes despectui habent _ all the spanish and italian tragedy i have yet see be writ in rhyme for the french i do not name they : because it be the fate of we countryman to admit little of theirs among we but the basest of they man the extravagancy of they fashion and the frippery of they merchandise shakespeare who -lrb- with some error not to be avoid in that age -rrb- have undoubtedly a larger soul of poesy than ever any of we nation be the first who -lrb- to shun the pain of continual rhyming -rrb- invent that kind of write which we call blank verse -lrb- _ dryden be here wrong as to fact lord surrey write the earliest _ print _ english blank verse in he fourth book of the _ aeneid _ print in _ 1548 -rrb- but the french more properly _ prose mesure _ : into which the english tongue so naturally slide that in write prose ` tis hardly to be avoid and therefore i admire -lrb- _ marvel that _ -rrb- some man should perpetually stumble in a way so easy : and invert the order of they word constantly close they line with verb which though commend sometimes in write latin yet we be whipt at westminster if we use it twice together i know some who if they be to write in blank verse _ sir i ask you pardon _ would think it sound more heroically to write _ sir i you pardon ask _ i should judge he to have little command of english whom the necessity of a _ rhyme _ should force upon this rock though sometimes it can not be easily avoid and indeed this be the only inconvenience with which rhyme can be charge this be that which make they say rhyme be not natural it be only so when the poet either make a vicious choice of word or place they for rhyme 's sake so unnaturally as no man would in ordinary speaking but when ` tis so judiciously order that the first word in the verse seem to beget the second and that the next till that become the last word in the line which in the negligence of prose would be so : it must then be grant rhyme have all advantage of prose besides its own but the excellence and dignity of it be never fully know till mr waller teach it he first make write easily a art : first show we to conclude the sense most commonly in distich which in the verse of those before he run on for so many line together that the reader be out of breath to overtake it this sweetness of mr waller 's lyric poesy be afterwards follow in the epic by sir john denham in he _ cooper 's hill _ a poem which you lordship know for the majesty of the style be and ever will be the exact standard of good writing but if we owe the invention of it to mr waller we be acknowledge for the noblest use of it to sir william d'avenant who at once bring it upon the stage and make it perfect in _ the siege of rhode _ the advantage which rhyme have over blank verse be so many that it be lose time to name they sir philip sidney in he _ defense of poesy _ give we one which in my opinion be not the least considerable : i mean _ the help it bring to memory _ which rhyme so knit up by the affinity of sound that by remember the last word in one line we often call to mind both the verse then in the quickness of repartee which in discoursive scene fall very often : it have so particular a grace and be so aptly suit to they that _ the sudden smartness of the answer and the sweetness of the rhyme set off the beauty of each other _ but that benefit which i consider most in it because i have not seldom find it be that _ it bound and circumscribe the fancy _ for imagination in a poet be a faculty so wild and lawless that like a high range spaniel it must have clog tie to it lest it outrun the judgement the great easiness of blank verse render the poet too luxuriant he be tempt to say many thing which might better be omit or at least shut up in fewer word but when the difficulty of artful rhyming be interpose where the poet commonly confines he sense to he couplet and must contrive that sense into such word that the rhyme shall naturally follow they not they the rhyme -lrb- pp 571 581 -rrb- : the fancy then give leisure to the judgement to come in which see so heavy a tax impose be ready to cut off all unnecessary expense this last consideration have already answer a objection which some have make that rhyme be only a embroidery of sense to make that which be ordinary in itself pass for excellent with less examination but certainly that which most regulate the fancy and give the judgement its busiest employment be like -lrb- ly -rrb- to bring forth the richest and clearest thought the poet examine that most which he produceth with the greatest leisure and which he know must pass the severest test of the audience because they be aptest to have it ever in they memory : as the stomach make the best concoction when it strictly embrace the nourishment and take account of every little particle as it pass through but as the best medicine may lose they virtue by be ill apply so be it with verse if a fit subject be not choose for it neither must the argument alone but the character and person be great and noble : otherwise as scaliger say of claudian the poet will be _ ignobiliore materium depressus _ the scene which -lrb- in my opinion -rrb- most commend it be those of argumentation and discourse on the result of which the do or not do -lrb- of -rrb- some considerable action should depend but my lord though i have more to say upon this subject yet i must remember ` tis you lordship to whom i speak : who have much better commend this way by you write _ in _ it than i can do by write _ for _ it where my reason can not prevail i be sure you lordship 's example must you rhetoric have gain my cause as least the greatest part of my design have already succeed to my wish : which be to interest so noble a person in the quarrel and withal to testify to the world how happy i esteem myself in the honor of be my lord you lordship 's most humble and most obedient servant john dryden the honorable sir robert howard auditor of the exchequer preface to _ four new play _ -lrb- licensed 7 march 1665 print the same year -rrb- _ to the reader _ there be none more sensible than i be how great a charity the most ingenious may need that expose they private wit to a public judgement since the same phancy from whence the thought proceed must probably be kind to its own issue this render man no perfecter judge of they own writings than father be of they own child : who find out that wit in they which another discern not and see not those error which be evident to the unconcerned nor be this self kindness more fatal to man in they writings than in they action every man be a greater flatterer to himself than he know how to be to another : otherwise it be impossible that thing of such distant nature should find they own author so equally kind in they affection to they and man so different in part and virtue should rest equally contented in they own opinion this apprehension add to that greater -lrb- one -rrb- which i have of my own weakness may i hope incline the reader to believe I when i assure he that these folly be make public as much against my inclination as judgement but be pursue with so many solicitation of mr herringman 's -lrb- _ the publisher _ -rrb- and have receive civility from he if it be possible exceed he importunity : i at last yield to prefer that which he believe he interest before that which i apprehended my own disadvantage consider withal that he might pretend it would be a real loss to he : and could be but a imaginary prejudice to I : since thing of this nature though never so excellent or never so mean have seldom prove the foundation of man 's new build fortune or the ruin of they old it be the fate of poetry though of no other good part to be wholly separate from interest : and there be few that know I but will easily believe i be not much concern in a unprofitable reputation this clear account i have give the reader of this seeming contradiction to offer that _ be a excellent _ comment _ and i believe restore to we that second book of he -lrb- _ ie aristotle _ -rrb- concern _ comedy _ which be want in he out of these two -lrb- author -rrb- have be extract the famous rule which the french call _ des trois units _ or ` the three unity ' which ought to be observe in every _ regular _ play namely of time place and action the unity of time they comprehend in twenty-four hour _ the compass of a natural day _ or as near it as can be contrived and the reason of it be obvious to every one that _ the time _ of the feigned action or fable of the play _ should be proportion _ as near as can be _ to the duration of that time in which it be represent _ since therefore all play be act on the theater in a space of time _ much within _ the compass of twenty-four hour that play be to be think the _ nearest imitation _ of nature whose plot or action be confine within that time and by the same rule which conclude this general proportion of time it follow _ that all the part of it be to be equally subdivide _ as namely that one act take not up the suppose time of half a day which be out of proportion to the rest since the other four be then to be straiten within the compass of the remain half : for it be unnatural that one act which be speak or write be not longer than the rest should be suppose longer by the audience ` tis therefore the poet 's duty to take care _ that no act _ should be imagine to _ exceed the time in which it be represent on the stage _ and that the interval and inequality of time be suppose to fall out _ between _ the act this rule of time how well it have be observe by the ancient most of they play will witness you see they in they tragedy -lrb- wherein to follow this rule be certainly most difficult -rrb- from the very beginning of they play fall close into that part of the story which they intend for the action or principal object of it : leave the former part to be deliver by narration so that they set the audience as it be at the post where the race be to be conclude : and save they the tedious expectation of see the poet set out and ride the beginning of the course you behold he not till he be in sight of the goal and just upon you for the second unity which be that of place the ancient mean by it _ that the scene _ -lrb- locality -rrb- _ ought to be continue _ through the play _ in the same place where it be lay in the begin _ for _ the stage _ on which it be represent _ be but one and the same place it isunnatural to conceive it many and those far distant from one another _ i will not deny but by the variation of paint scene -lrb- _ scenery be introduce about this time into the english theater by sir william d'avenant and betterton the actor : see vol ii p 278 _ -rrb- the fancy which in these cast will contribute to its own deceit may sometimes imagine it several place upon some appearance of probability : yet it still carry _ the greater likelihood of truth _ if those place be suppose so near each other as in the same town or city which may all be comprehend under the larger denomination of one place for a greater distance will bear no proportion to the _ shortness of time which be allot in the act _ to pass from one of they to another for the observation of this next to the ancient the french be most to be commend they tie themselves so strictly to the unity of place that you never see in any of they play a scene -lrb- _ locality _ -rrb- change in the middle of a act if the act begin in a garden a street or -lrb- a -rrb- chamber ` tis end in the same place and that you may know it to be the same the stage be so supply with person that it be never empty all the time he that enter the second have business with he who be on before and before the second quit the stage a third appear who have business with he this corneille call _ la liaison des scene _ ` the continuity or join of the scene ' : and it be a good mark of a well contrived play when all the person be know to each other and every one of they have some affair with all the rest as for the third unity which be that of action the ancient mean no other by it than what the logician do by they _ fini _ the end or scope of any action that which be the first in intention and last in execution now the poet be to aim at _ one great and complete action _ to the carry on of which all thing in the play even the very obstacle be to be subservient and the reason of this be as evident as any of the former for two action equally labor and drive on by the writer would destroy the unity of the poem it would be no longer one play but two not but that there may be many action in a play -lrb- as ben johnson have observe in he _ discovery _ -rrb- but they must be all subservient to the great one which we language happily express in the name of under plot such as in terence 's _ eunuch _ be the deference and reconcilement of _ thai _ and _ phaedrium _ which be not the chief business of the play but promote the marriage of _ choerea _ and _ chreme 's sister _ principally intend by the poet ` there ought to be but one action ' say corneille ` that be one complete action which leave the mind of the audience in a full repose ' but this can not be bring to pass but by many other imperfect one which conduce to it and hold the audience in a delightful suspense of what will be if by these rule -lrb- to omit many other draw from the precept and practice of the ancient -rrb- we should judge we modern play ` tis probable that few of they would endure the trial that which should be the business of a day take up in some of they a age instead of one action they be the epitome of a man 's life and for one spot of ground which the stage should represent we be sometimes in more country play we have reason to conjecture they sing more than five time aristotle indeed divide the integral part of a play into four firstly the _ protasis _ or entrance which give light only to the character of the person and proceeds very little into any part of the action secondly the _ epitasis _ or work up of the plot where the play grow warmer the design or action of it be draw on and you see something promising that it will come to pass thirdly the _ catastasis _ or counter-turn which destroy that expectation embroil the action in new difficulty and leave you far distant from that hope in which it find you : as you may have observe in a violent stream resist by a narrow passage it turn round to a eddy and carry back the water with more swiftness than it bring they on lastly the there be not anything more unreasonable to my judgement than the attempt to infringe the liberty of opinion by rule so little demonstrative to shew therefore upon what ill grounds they dictate law for dramatic poesy i shall endeavor to make it evident that there be no such thing as what they all pretend -lrb- p 592 -rrb- for if strictly and duly weigh ` tis as impossible for one stage to represent two house or two room truly as two country or kingdom and as impossible that five hour or four and twenty hour should be two hour and a half as that a thousand hour or year should be less than what they be or the greatest part of time to be comprehend in the less for _ all _ be impossible they be none of they nearest the truth or nature of what they present for impossibility be all equal and admit no degree and then if all those poet that have so fervently labor to give rule as maxim would but be please to abbreviate or endure to hear they reason reduce into one strict definition it must with two distant city in the same country or two remote country in the same universe imagination in a man or reasonable creature be suppose to participate of reason and when that govern -lrb- as it do in the belief of fiction -rrb- reason be not destroy but mislead or blind that can prescribe to the reason during the time of the representation somewhat like a weak belief of what it see and hear and reason suffer itself to be so hoodwink that it may better enjoy the pleasure of the fiction : but it be never so wholly make a captive as to be draw headlong into a persuasion of those thing which be most remote from probability ` tis in that case a free bear subject not a slave it will contribute willingly its assent as far as it see convenient : but will not be force now there be a greater vicinity in nature betwixt two room than betwixt two house betwixt two house than betwixt two city and so of the rest reason therefore can sooner be lead by imagination to step from one room to another than to walk to two distant house : and yet rather to go thither than to fly like a witch through the air and be hurry from one region to another fancy and reason go hand in hand the first can not leave the last behind : and though fancy when it see the wide gulf would venture over as the nimbler yet it be withhold by reason which will refuse to take the leap when the distance over it appear too large if ben johnson himself will remove the scene from rome into tuscany in the same act and from thence return to rome in the scene which immediate follow reason will consider there be no proportionable allowance of time to perform the journey and therefore will choose to stay at home so then the less change of place there be the less time be take up in transport the person of the drama with analogy to reason : and in that analogy or resemblance of fiction to truth consist the excellency of the play for what else concern the unity of place i have already give my opinion of it in my _ essay _ that there be a latitude to be allow to it as several place in the same town or city or place adjacent to each other in the same country which may all be comprehend under the larger denomination of one place yet with this restriction the nearer and fewer those imaginary place be the greater resemblance they will have to truth : and reason which can not _ make _ they one will be more easily lead to _ suppose _ they so what have be say of the unity of place may easily be apply to that of time i grant it to be impossible that _ the greater part of time should be comprehend in the less _ that _ twenty-four hour should be crowd into three _ but there be no necessity of that supposition for as place so time relate to a play be either imaginary or real the real be comprehend in those three hour more or less in the space of which the play be represent the imaginary be that which be suppose to be take up in the representation as twenty-four hour more or less now no man ever could suppose that twenty-four _ real _ hour could be include in the space of three : but where be the absurdity of affirm that the feigned business of twenty-four _ imagine _ hour may not more naturally be represent in the compass of three _ real _ hour than the like feigned business of twenty-four year in the same proportion of real time for the _ proportion _ be always real and much nearer by he permission of twenty-four to three than of 4000 to it i be almost fearful of illustrate _ anything _ by similitude lest he should confute it for a argument : yet i think the comparison of a glass will discover very aptly the fallacy of he argument both concern time and place the strength of he reason depend on this that the less can not comprehend the greater i have already answer that we need not suppose it do i say not that the less can _ comprehend _ the greater but only that it may _ represent _ it as in a mirror of half a yard -lrb- in -rrb- diameter a whole room and many person in it may be see at once : not that it can _ comprehend _ that room or those person but that it _ represent they to the sight _ but the author of _ the duke of lerma _ be to be excuse for he declare against the unity of time for if i be not much mistaken he be a interessed -lrb- _ interested _ -rrb- person the time of that play take up so many year as the favor of the duke of lerma continue : nay the second and third act include all the time of he prosperity which be a great part of the reign of philip iii for in the beginning of the second act he be not yet a favorite and before the end of the third be in disgrace i say not this with the least design of limit the stage too servilely to twenty-four hour : however he be please to tax I with dogmatize in that point in my dialogue as i before hint several person maintain they several opinion one of they indeed who support the cause of the french poesy say how strict they be in that particular -lrb- p 531 -rrb- but he who answer in behalf of we nation be willing to give more latitude to the rule and cite the word of corneille himself complain against the severity of it and observe what beauty it banish from the stage page 44 of my _ essay _ in few word my own opinion be this and i willingly submit it to my adversary when he will please impartially to consider it that the imaginary time of every play ought to be contrived into as narrow a compass as the nature of the plot the quality of the person and variety of accident will allow in comedy i would not exceed twenty-four or thirty hour for the plot accident and person of comedy be small and may be naturally turn in a little compass but in tragedy the design be weighty and the person great therefore there will naturally be require a greater space of time in which to move they and this though ben johnson have not tell we yet ` tis manifestly he opinion for you see that to he comedy he allow generally but twenty-four hour : to he two tragedy _ sejanus _ and _ catiline _ a much larger time though he draw both of they into as narrow a compass as he can for he show you only the latter end of _ sejanus _ he favor and the conspiracy of _ catiline _ already ripe and just break out into action but as it be a error on the one side to make too great a disproportion betwixt the _ imaginary _ time of the play and the _ real _ time of its representation : so on the other side ` tis a oversight to compress the accident of a play into a narrower compass than that in which they could naturally be produce of this last error the french be seldom guilty because the thinness of they plot prevent they from it : but few englishman except ben johnson have ever make a plot with variety of design in it include in twenty-four hour which be altogether natural for this reason i prefer the _ silent woman _ before all other play i think justly : as i do its author in judgement above all other poet yet of the two i think that error the most pardonable which in too straight a compass crowd together many accident : since it produce more variety and consequently more pleasure to the audience and because the nearness of proportion betwixt the imaginary and real time do speciously cover the compression of the accident thus i have endeavor to answer the _ meaning _ of he argument for as he draw it i humbly conceive it be none as will appear by he proposition and the proof of it he proposition be this _ if strictly and duly weigh ` tis as impossible for one stage to present two room or house as two country or kingdom & c _ and he proof this _ for all be impossible they be none of they nearest the truth or nature of what they present _ here you see instead of a proof or reason there be only a _ petitio principius _ for in plain word he sense be this two thing be as impossible as one another : because they be both equally impossible but he take those two thing to be _ grant _ as impossible which he ought to have _ prove _ such before he have proceed to prove they equally impossible he should have make out first that it be impossible for one stage to represent two house and then have go forward to prove that it be as equally impossible for a stage to present two house as two country after all this the very absurdity to which he would reduce I be none at all for he only drive at this that if he argument be true i must then acknowledge that there be degree in impossibility which i easily grant he without dispute and if i mistake not aristotle and the school be of my opinion for there be some thing which be absolutely impossible and other which be only so _ ex parte _ as ` tis absolutely impossible for a thing _ to be _ and _ not to be _ at the same time : but for a stone to move naturally upward be only impossible _ ex parte materiae _ but it be not impossible for the first mover to alter the nature of it he last assault like that of a frenchman be most feeble for where i have observe that none have be violent against verse but such only as have not attempt it or have succeed ill in they attempt -lrb- pp 503539561578 -rrb- he will need accord to he usual custom improve my observation into a argument that he might have the glory to confute it but i lay my observation at he foot : as i do my pen which i have often employ willingly in he deserve commendation and now most unwillingly against he judgement for he person and part i honor they as much as any man living : and have have so many particular obligation to he that i should be very ungrateful if i do not acknowledge they to the world but i give not the first occasion of this difference in opinion in my _ epistle dedicatory _ before my _ rival lady _ -lrb- pp 487-493 -rrb- i say somewhat in behalf of verse : which he be please to answer in he _ preface _ to he _ play _ -lrb- pp 494-500 -rrb- that occasion my reply in my _ essay _ -lrb- pp 501-572 -rrb- : and that reply beget he rejoinder in he _ preface _ to _ the duke of lerma _ -lrb- pp 573-578 -rrb- but as i be the last who take up arm i will be the first to lay they down for what i have here write i submit it wholly to he -lrb- p 561 -rrb- and if i do not hereafter answer what may be object to this paper i hope the world will not impute it to any other reason than only the due respect which i have for so noble a opponent thoma ellwood _ relation with john milton _ i mention before that when i be a boy i make some good progress in learning and lose it all again before i come to be a man : nor be i rightly sensible of my loss therein until i come amongst the quaker but then i both see my loss and lament it and apply myself with the utmost diligence at all leisure time to recover it : so false i find that charge to be which in those time be cast as a reproach upon the quaker that they despise and decry all human learning because they deny it to be _ essentially necessary _ to a gospel ministry which be one of the controversy of those time but though i toil hard and spare no pain to regain what once i have be master of yet i find it a matter of so great difficulty that i be ready to say as the noble eunuch to philip in another case how can i unless i have some man to guide I this i have formerly complain of to my especial friend isaac penington but now more earnestly which put he upon consider and contrive a means for my assistance he have a intimate acquaintance with dr paget a physician of note in london and he with john milton a gentleman of great note in learn throughout the learn world for the accurate piece he have write on various subject and occasion this person have fill a public station in the former time live now a private and retired life in london : and have wholly lose he sight keep a man to read to he which usually be the son of some gentleman of he acquaintance whom in kindness he take to improve in he learn thus by the mediation of my friend isaac penington with dr paget and of dr paget with john milton be i admit to come to he : not as a servant to he -lrb- which at that time he need not -rrb- nor to be in the house with he but only to have the liberty of come to he house at certain hour when i would and to read to he what book he should appoint I which be all the favor i desire but this be a matter which would require some time to bring it about i in the meanwhile return to my father 's house -lrb- at crowell -rrb- in oxfordshire i have before receive direction by letter from my eldest sister write by my father 's command to put off -lrb- _ dispose of _ -rrb- what cattle he have leave about he house and to discharge he servant which i have do at the time call michaelma -lrb- 1661 -rrb- before so that all that winter when i be at home i live like a hermit all alone have a pretty large house and nobody in it but myself at night especially but a elderly woman whose father have be a old servant to the family come every morning and make my bed and do what else i have occasion for she to do : till i fall ill of the small-pox and then i have she with I and the nurse but now understand by letter from my sister that my father do not intend to return and settle there i make off -lrb- _ sell _ -rrb- those provision which be in the house that they might not be spoil when i be go : and because they be what i should have spend if i have tarry there i take the money make of they to myself for my support at london if the project succeed for my go thither this do i commit the care of the house to a tenant of my father 's who live in the town and take my leave of crowell go up to my sure friend isaac penington again where understand that the mediation use for my admittance to john milton have succeed so well that i might come when i would : i hasten to london -lrb- _ in the spring of 1662 _ -rrb- and in the first place go to wait upon he he receive I courteously as well for the sake of dr paget who introduce I as of isaac penington who recommend I : to both of whom he bear a good respect and have inquire diver thing of I with respect to my former progression in learn he dismiss I to provide myself of such accommodation as might be most suitable to my future study i go therefore and take myself a lodging as near to he house which be then in jewin street as conveniently as i could and from thenceforward go every day in the afternoon except on the first day of the week and sit by he in he dining-room read to he in such book in the latin tongue as he please to hear I read at my first sit to read to he observe that i use the english pronounciation he tell I if i would have the benefit of the latin tongue not only to read and understand latin author but to converse with foreigner either abroad or at home i must learn the foreign pronounciation to this i consent he instruct I how to sound the vowel so different -lrb- ly -rrb- from the common pronounciation use by the english who speak _ anglice _ they latin that -lrb- with some few other variation in sound some consonant : in particular case -lrb- s -rrb- as _ c _ before _ e _ or _ i _ like _ ch _ _ sc _ before _ i _ like _ sh _ & c -rrb- the latin thus speak seem as different from that which be deliver as the english generally speak it as if it be another language i have before during my retired life at my father 's by unwearied diligence and industry so far recover the rule of grammar -lrb- in which i have once be very ready -rrb- that i could both read a latin author and after a sort hammer out he meaning but this change of pronounciation prove a new difficulty to I it be now harder for I to read than it be before to understand when read but _ labor omnium vincit improbus _ incessant pain the end obtain and so do i : which make my read the more acceptable to my master he on the other hand perceive with what earnest desire i pursued learning give I not only all the encouragement but all the help he could for have a curious ear he understand by my tone when i understand what i read and when i do not and accordingly would stop I examine I and open the most difficult passage thus i go on for about six week ' time read to he in the afternoon and exercise myself with my own book in my chamber in the forenoon i be sensible of a improvement but alas i have fix my study in a wrong place london and i could never agree for health my lung as i suppose be too tender to bear the sulphurous air of that city so that i soon begin to droop and in less than two month ' time i be fain to leave both my study and the city and return into the country to preserve life and much ado i have to get thither i choose to go down to wiccombe and to john rance 's house there : both as he be a physician and he wife a honest hearty discreet and grave matron whom i have a very good esteem of and who i know have a good regard for I there i lay ill a considerable time and to that degree of weakness that scarcely any who see I expect my life -lrb- _ that i should live _ -rrb- : but the lord be both gracious to I in my illness and be please to raise I up again that i might serve he in my generation as soon as i have recover so much strength as to be fit to travel i obtain of my father -lrb- who be then at he house in crowell to dispose of some thing he have there and who in my illness have come to see I -rrb- so much money as would clear all charge in the house for physic food and attendance : and have fully discharge all i take leave of my friend in that family and town and return -lrb- _ in October 1662 _ -rrb- to my study at london i be very kindly receive by my master who have conceive so good a opinion of I that my conversation i find be acceptable to he and he seem heartily glad of my recovery and return : and into we old method of study we fall again i read to he and he explain to I as occasion require but as if learning have be a forbid fruit to I scarce be i well settle in my work before i meet with another diversion -lrb- _ hindrance _ -rrb- which turn I quite out of my work for a sudden storm arise -lrb- from i know not what surmise of a plot and thereby danger to the government -rrb- the meeting of dissenter such i mean as could be find -lrb- which the trained band of the city rush in with noise and clamor : be lead by one who be call major rosewell : a apothecary if i misremember not and at that time under the ill name of a papist -lrb- so the friend there with ellwood be take and send to bridewell till the 19th December following : when they be take to newgate expect to be call at the old bailey session : but not be call be send back to bridewell again on the 29th December they be bring up at the session and refuse to swear be all commit to the common side of newgate but that prison be so full they be send back to bridewell again then we have the follow extraordinary circumstance -rrb- have make up we pack and take we leave of we friend whom we be to leave behind we take we bundle on we shoulder and walk two and two a breast through the old bailey into fleet street and so to old bridewell and it be about the middle of the afternoon and the street pretty full of people both the shopkeeper at they door and passenger in the way would stop we and ask we what we be and whither we be go and when we have tell they we be prisoner go from one prison to another -lrb- from newgate to bridewell -rrb- what say they without a keeper no say we for we word which we have give be we keeper some thereupon would advise we not to go to prison but to go home but we tell they we could not do so we could suffer for we testimony but could not fly from it i do not remember we have any abuse offer we but be generally pity by the people when we be come to bridewell we be not put up into the great room in which we have be before but into a low room in another fair court which have a pump in the middle of it and here we be not shut up as before but have the liberty of the court to walk in and of the pump to wash and drink at and indeed we might easily have go quite away if we would there be a passage through the court into the street : but we be true and steady prisoner and look upon this liberty arise from they confidence in we to be a kind of _ parole _ upon we so that both conscience and honor stand now engage for we true imprisonment and this privilege we enjoy by the indulgence of we keeper whose heart god dispose to favor we so that both the master and he porter be very civil and kind to we and have be so indeed all along for when we be shut up before the porter would readily let some of we go home in a evening and stay at home till next morning which be a great conveniency to man of trade and business which i be free from forbore ask for myself that i might not hinder other under this easy restraint we lay till the court sate at the old bailey again and then -lrb- whether it be that the heat of the storm be somewhat abate or by what other means providence work it i know not -rrb- we be call to the bar and without further question discharge whereupon we return to bridewell again and have raise some money among we and therewith gratify both the master and he porter for they kindness to we we spend some time in a solemn meeting to return we thankful acknowledgment to the lord both for he preservation of we in prison and deliverance of we out of it and then take a solemn farewell of each other we depart with bag and baggage -lrb- _ at the end of January 1663 _ -rrb- -lrb- thus by such magnificent patience under arbitrary injustice these invincible quaker shame the reckless crime which in those day go by the name of the law and such story as ellwood 's _ life _ and george fox 's _ journal _ abound with like splendid victory of patience by man who be incapable of tell a lie or of intentionally break they word john bunyan 's imprisonment at this time be much of the same kind as ellwood 's as soon as the keeper of bedford gaol find he could trust he -rrb- be now at liberty i tongue this question be not more unexpected than surprising to I and the more because it seem directly to thwart my former purpose and undertaking of endeavor to improve myself by follow my study with my master milton which this would give at least a present diversion from and for how long i could not foresee but the sense i have of the manifold obligation i lay under to these worthy friend of mine shut out all reasoning and disposed my mind to a absolute resignation to they desire that i might testify my gratitude by a willingness to do they any friendly service that i could be capable of and though i question my ability to carry on that work to its due height and proportion yet as that be not propose but a initiation only by accidence into grammar i consent to the proposal as a present expedient till a more qualified person should be find without further treaty or mention of term between we than that of mutual friendship and to render this digression from my own study the less uneasy to my mind i recollect and often think of that rule of lilly -- _ quus docet indocto licet indoctissimus esset ipse brevus reliqui doctior esse queat _ he that th ` unlearned doth teach may husband 's kind usage : but we who at the cost of we friend have a proof of he kindness be too wary to be draw in by the fair word of a woman : and therefore tell she we would not settle anywhere till she husband come home and then would have a free prison wheresoever he put we accordingly walk all together into the court of the prison in which be a well of very good water and have beforehand send he come to we he hide he tooth and put on a shew of kindness seem much trouble that we should sit there abroad -lrb- _ in the open air _ -rrb- especially he old friend mr penington and thereupon strive which of they should take from the traveller he cloak -rrb- have like the wind try rough boisterous violent means to we friend before but in vain resolve now to imitate the sun and shine as
