on 2nd July 1670 -rrb- advice to a young reviewer with a specimen of the art 1807 advice to a young reviewer & c you be now about to enter on a profession which have the means of do much good to society and scarcely any temptation to do harm you may encourage genius you may chastise superficial arrogance expose falsehood correct error and guide the taste and opinion of the age in no small degree by the book you praise and recommend and this too may be do without run the risk of make any enemy or subject yourself to be call to account for you criticism however severe while you name be unknown you person be invulnerable : at the same time you aim be sure for you may take it at you leisure and you blow fall heavier than those of any writer whose name be give or who be simply anonymous there be a mysterious authority in the plural _ we _ which no single name whatever may be its reputation can acquire and under the sanction of this impose style you stricture you praise and you dogma will command universal attention and be receive as the fruit of unite talent act on one common principle -- as the judgment of a tribunal who decide only on mature deliberation and who protect the interest of literature with unceasing vigilance such be the high importance of that office and such its opportunity i can not bestow a few hour of leisure better than in furnish you with some hint for the more easy and effectual discharge of it : hint which be i confess loosely throw together but which be the result of long experience and of frequent reflection and comparison and if anything should strike you at first sight as rather equivocal in point of morality or deficient in liberality and feeling i beg you will suppress all such scruple and consider they as the offspring of a contract education and narrow way of think which a little intercourse with the world and sober reasoning will speedily overcome now as in the conduct of life nothing be more to be desire than some govern principle of action to which all other principle and motive must be make subservient so in the art of review i would lay down as a fundamental position which you must never lose sight of and which must be the mainspring of all you criticism -- _ write what will sell _ to this golden rule every minor canon must be subordinate and must be either immediately deducible from it or at least be make consistent with it be not stagger at the sound of a precept which upon examination will be find as honest and virtuous as it be discreet i have already sketch out the great service which it be in you power to render mankind but all you effort will be unavailing if man do not read what you write you utility therefore it be plain depend upon you popularity and popularity can not be attain without humor the taste and inclination of man be assure that by a similar train of sound and judicious reasoning the conscience of thousand in public life be daily quiet it be better for the state that they party should govern than any other the good which they can effect by the exercise of power be infinitely greater than any which could arise from a rigid adherence to certain subordinate moral precept which therefore should be violate without scruple whenever they stand in the way of they lead purpose he who stick at these can never act a great part in the world and be not fit to act it if he could such maxim may be very useful in ordinary affair and for the guidance of ordinary man : but when we mount into the sphere of public utility we must adopt more enlarged principle and not suffer ourselves to be cramped and fetter by petty notion of right and moral duty when you have reconcile yourself to this liberal way of think you will find many inferior advantage result from it which at first do not enter into you consideration in particular it will greatly lighten you labor to _ follow _ the public taste instead of take upon you to _ direct _ it the task of pleasing be at all time easier than that of instruct : at least it do not stand in need of painful research and preparation and may be effect in general by a little vivacity of manner and a dexterous morigeration -lrb- _ compliance or obsequiousness _ -rrb- as lord bacon call it to the humor and frailty of man you responsibility too be thereby much lessen justice and candor can only be require of you so far as they coincide with this main principle : and a little experience will convince you that these be not the happiest means of accomplish you purpose it have be idly say that a reviewer act in a judicial capacity and that he conduct should be regulate by the same rule by which the judge of a civil court be govern : that he should rid himself of every bias be patient cautious sedate and rigidly impartial that he should not seek to shew off himself and should check every disposition to enter into the case as a partizan such be the language of superficial thinker but in reality there be no analogy between the two case a judge be promote to that office by the authority of the state a reviewer by he own the former be independent of control and may therefore freely follow the dictate of he own conscience : the latter depend for he very bread upon the breath of public opinion the great law of self-preservation therefore point out to he a different line of action besides as we have already observe if he cease to please he be no longer read and consequently be no longer useful in a court of justice too the part of amusing the bystander rest with the counsel : in the case of criticism if the reviewer himself do not undertake it who will instead of vainly aspire to the gravity of a magistrate i would advise he when he sit down to write to place himself in the imaginary situation of a cross-examining pleader he may comment in a vein of agreeable irony upon the profession the manner of life the look dress or even the name of the witness he be examine : when he have raise a contemptuous opinion of he in the mind of the court he may proceed to draw answer from he capable of a ludicrous turn and he may carve and garble these to he own liking this mode of proceed you will find most practicable in poetry where the boldness of the image or the delicacy of thought -lrb- for which the reader 's mind be prepare in the original -rrb- will easily be make to appear extravagant or affect if judiciously single out and detach from the group to which it belong again since much depend upon the rhythm and the terseness of expression -lrb- both of which be sometimes destroy by drop a single word or transpose a phrase -rrb- i have know much advantage arise from _ not _ quote in the form of a literal extract : but give a brief summary in prose of the contents of a poetical passage and interlard you own language with occasional phrase of the poem mark with inverted comma these and a thousand other little expedient by which the art of quiz and banter flourish practice will soon teach you if it should be necessary to transcribe a dull passage not very fertile in topic of humor and raillery you may introduce it as a favourable specimen of the author 's manner few people be aware of the powerful effect of what be philosophically term association without any positive violation of truth the whole dignity of a passage may be undermine by contrive to raise some vulgar and ridiculous notion in the mind of the reader : and language teem with example of word by which the same idea be express with the difference only that one excite a feeling of respect the other of contempt thus you may call a fit of melancholy the sulk resentment a pet a steed a nag a feast a junketing sorrow and affliction whining and blubber by transfer the term peculiar to one state of society to analogous situation and character in another the same object be attain a drill serjeant or a cat and nine tail in the trojan war a lesbo smack put into the piraeus the penny post of jerusalem and other combination of the like nature which when you have a little indulged in that vein of thought will readily suggest themselves never fail to raise a smile if not immediately at the expense of the author yet entirely destructive of that frame of mind which he poem require in order to be relish i have dwell the longer on this branch of literature because you be chiefly to look here for material of fun and irony voyage and travels indeed be no barren ground and you must seldom let a number of you _ review _ go abroad without a article of this description the charm of this species of write so universally feel arise chiefly from its unite narrative with information the interest we take in the story can only be keep alive by minute incident and occasional detail which put we in possession of the traveller 's feeling he hope he fear he disappointment and he pleasure at the same time the thirst for knowledge and love of novelty be gratify by continual information respect the people and country he visit if you wish therefore to run down the book you have only to play off these two part against each other when the writer 's object be to satisfy the first inclination you be to thank he for communicate to the world such valuable fact as whether he lose he way in the night or sprain he ankle or have no appetite for he dinner if he be busy about describe the mineralogy natural history agriculture trade etc of a country : you may mention a hundred book from whence the same information may be obtain and deprecate the practice of empty old musty folio into new quarto to gratify that sickly taste for a smattering about everything which distinguish the present age in work of science and recondite learn the task you have undertake will not be so difficult as you may imagine table of contents and index be bless help in the hand of a reviewer but more than all the preface be the field from which he richest harvest be to be gather in the preface the author usually give a summary of what have be write on the same subject before he acknowledge the assistance he have receive from different source and the reason of he dissent from former writer he confess that certain part have be less attentively consider than other and that information have come to he hand too late to be make use of he point out many thing in the composition of he work which he think may provoke animadversion and endeavor to defend or palliate he own practice here then be a fund of wealth for the reviewer lie upon the very surface if he know anything of he business he will turn all these material against the author : carefully suppress the source of he information and as if draw from the store of he own mind long ago lay up for this very purpose if the author 's reference be correct a great point be gain for by consult a few passage of the original work it will be easy to discuss the subject with the air of have a previous knowledge of the whole you chief vantage ground be that you may fasten upon any position in the book you be review and treat it as principal and essential when perhaps it be of little weight in the main argument : but by allot a large share of you criticism to it the reader will naturally be lead to give it a proportionate importance and to consider the merit of the treatise at issue upon that single question if anybody complain that the greater and more valuable part remain unnoticed you answer be that it be impossible to pay attention to all and that you duty be rather to prevent the propagation of error than to lavish praise upon that which if really excellent will work its way in the world without you help indeed if the plan of you _ review _ admit of selection you have better not meddle with work of deep research and original speculation such as have already attract much notice and can not be treat superficially without fear of be find out the time require for make yourself thoroughly master of the subject be so great that you may depend upon it they will never pay for the review they be generally the fruit of long study and of talent concentrate in the steady pursuit of one object : it be not likely therefore that you can throw much new light on a question of this nature or even plausibly combat the author 's proposition in the course of a few hour which be all you can well afford to devote to they and without accomplish one or the other of these point you _ review _ will gain no celebrity and of course no good will be do enough have be say to give you some insight into the facility with which you new employment abound i will only mention one more because of its extensive and almost universal application to all branch of literature the topic i mean which by the old rhetorician be call -lrb- greek : _ ex enantion _ -rrb- that be when a work excel in one quality you may blame it for not have the opposite for instance if the biographical sketch of a literary character be minute and full of anecdote you may enlarge on the advantage of philosophical reflection and the superior mind require to give a judicious analysis of the opinion and work of deceased author on the contrary if the latter method be pursue by the biographer you can with equal ease extol the lively coloring and truth and interest of exact delineation and detail this topic you will perceive enter into style as well as matter where many virtue might be name _ which be incompatible _ : and whichever the author have prefer it will be the signal for you to launch forth on the praise of its opposite and continually to hold up that to you reader as the model of excellence in this species of write you will perhaps wonder why all my instruction be pointed towards the censure and not the praise of book but many reason might be give why it should be so the chief be that this part be both easier and will sell better let we hear the word of mr burke on a subject not very dissimilar : have any influence at all on human action thought or inclination : and whoever have not bent he study that way may be excuse for think so when he see in how wretched a manner this noble art be treat from no greater height than they own brain i intend in a short time to publish a large and rational defense of this art and therefore shall say no more in its justification at present than that it have be in all age defend by many learn man and among the rest by socrate himself whom i look upon as undoubtedly the wisest of uninspired mortal to which if we add that those who have condemn this art although otherwise learn have be such as either do not apply they study this way or at least do not succeed in they application they testimony will not be of much weight to its disadvantage since they be liable to the common objection of condemn what they do not understand nor be i at all offended or think it a injury to the art when i see the common dealer in it the _ student in astronomy _ the _ philomath _ and the rest of that tribe treat by wise man with the utmost scorn and contempt : but i rather wonder when i observe gentleman in the country rich enough to serve the nation in parliament pore in partridge 's _ almanack _ to find out the event of the year at home and abroad not daring to propose a hunting match unless gadbury or he have fix the weather i will allow either of the two i have mention or any other of the fraternity to be not only astrologer but conjurer too if i do not produce a hundred instance in all they _ almanack _ to convince any reasonable man that they do not so much as understand grammar and syntax that they be not able to spell any word out of the usual road nor even in they _ preface _ to write common sense or intelligible english then as they observation or prediction they be such as will suit any age or country in the world _ this month a certain great person will be threaten with death or sickness _ this the news paper will tell they for there we find at the end of the year that no month passeth without the death of some person of note : and it would be hard if it should be otherwise where there be at least two thousand person of note in this kingdom many of they old and the _ almanack _ maker have the liberty of choose the sickliest season of the year where he may fix he prediction again _ this month a eminent clergyman will be prefer _ of which there may be some hundred half of they with one foot in the grave then _ such a planet in such a house shew great machination plot and conspiracy that may in time be bring to light _ after which if we hear of any discovery the astrologer get the honor if not he prediction still stand good and at last _ god preserve king william from all he open and secret enemy amen _ when if the king should happen to have die the astrologer plainly foretell it otherwise it passeth but for the pious ejaculation of a loyal subject : although it unluckily happen in some of they _ almanack _ that poor king william be pray for many month after he be dead because it fall out that he die about the beginning of the year to mention no more of they impertinent prediction what have we to do with they advertisement about pill or they mutual quarrel in verse and prose of whig and tory wherewith the star have little to do have long observe and lament these and a hundred other abuse of this art too tedious to repeat i resolve to proceed in a new way which i doubt not will be to the general satisfaction of the kingdom i can this year produce but a specimen of what i design for the future : have employ the most part of my time in adjust and correct the calculation i make for some year past because i would offer nothing to the world of which i be not as fully satisfy as that i be now alive for these last two year i have not fail in above one or two particulars and those of no very great moment i exactly foretell the miscarriage at toulon -lrb- _ fruitlessly besiege by prince eugene between 26th July and 21st August _ 1707 -rrb- with all its particulars : and the loss of admiral -lrb- sir cloudesly -rrb- shovel -lrb- _ at the scilly isle on 22nd October _ 1707 -rrb- although i be mistake as to the day place that accident about thirty-six hour sooner than it happen but upon review my scheme i quickly find the cause of that error i likewise foretell the battle of almanza -lrb- _ 25th April _ 1707 -rrb- to the very day and hour with the loss on both side and the consequence thereof all which i shewed to some friend many month before they happen : that be i give they papers seal up to open in such a time after which they be at liberty to read they and there they find my prediction true in every article except one or two very minute as for the few follow prediction i now offer the world i forbore to publish they until i have peruse the several _ almanack _ for the year we be now enter upon i find they all in the usual strain and i beg the reader will compare they manner with mine and here i make bold to tell the world that i lay the whole credit of my art upon the truth of these prediction and i will be content that partridge and the rest of he clan may hoot I for a cheat and impostor if i fail in any single particular of moment i believe any man who read this paper -lrb- _ pamphlet _ -rrb- will look upon I to be at least a person of as much honesty and understanding as the common maker of _ almanack _ i do not lurk in the dark i be not wholly unknown to the world i have set my name at length to be a mark of infamy to mankind if they shall find i deceive they in one thing i must desire to be forgive : that i talk more sparingly of home affair as it would be imprudence to discover secret of state so it would be dangerous to my person : but in smaller matter and that as be not of public consequence i shall be very free : and the truth of my conjecture will as much appear from these as the other as for the most signal event abroad in france flander italy and spain : i shall make no scruple to predict they in plain term some of they be of importance and i hope i shall seldom mistake the day they will happen therefore i think good to inform the reader that i all along make use of the old style observe in england which i desire he will compare with that of the news papers at the time they relate the action i mention i must add one word more i know it have be the opinion of several learn -lrb- person -rrb- who think well enough of the true art of astrology that the star do only _ incline _ and not _ force _ the action or will of man : and therefore however i may proceed by right rule yet i can not in prudence so confidently assure that the event will follow exactly as i predict they i hope i have maturely consider this objection which in some case be of no little weight for example a man may by the influence of a overrule planet be dispose or incline to lust rage or avarice and yet by the force of reason overcome that evil influence and this be the case of socrate but the great event of the world usually depend upon number of man it can not be expect they should _ all _ unite to cross they inclination from pursue a general design wherein they unanimously agree besides the influence of the star reacheth to many action and event which be not in any way in the power of reason as sickness death and what we commonly call accident with many more needless to repeat but now it be time to proceed to my prediction : which i have begin to calculate from the time that the sun entereth into _ aries -lrb- April -rrb- _ and this i take to be properly the beginning of the natural year i pursue they to the time that he entereth _ libra -lrb- September -rrb- _ or somewhat more which be the busy period of the year the remainder i have not yet adjust upon account of several impediment needless here to mention besides i must remind the reader again that this be but a specimen of what i design in succeed year to treat more at large if i may have liberty and encouragement my first prediction be but a trifle yet i will mention it to shew how ignorant those sottish pretender to astrology be in they own concern it relateth to partridge the _ almanack _ maker i have consult the star of he nativity by my own rule and find he will infallibly die upon the 29th of march -lrb- 1708 -rrb- next about eleven at night of a rage fever therefore i advise he to consider of it and settle he affair in time the month of April will be observable for the death of many great person on the gentleman who read this paper will look upon it to be of the same last and mould with the common scribble that be every day hawk about my fortune have place I above the little regard of write for a 1708 -rrb- my lord in obedience to you lordship 's command as well as to satisfy my own curiosity i have for some day past inquire constantly after partridge the _ almanack _ maker : of whom it be foretell in mr bickerstaff 's _ prediction _ publish about a month ago that he should die the 29th instant about eleven at night of a rage fever i have some sort of knowledge of he when i be employ in the revenue because he use every year to present I with he _ almanack _ as he do other gentleman upon the score of some little gratuity we give he i see he accidentally once or twice about ten day before he die : and observe he begin very much to droop and languish although i hear he friend do not seem to apprehend he in any danger about two or three day ago he grow ill be confine first to he chamber and in a few hour after to he bed : where dr case and mr kirleus -lrb- _ two london quack _ -rrb- be send for to visit and to prescribe to he upon this intelligence i send thrice every day a servant or other to inquire after he health : and yesterday about four in the afternoon word be bring I that he be past hope upon which i prevail with myself to go and see he : partly out of commiseration : and i confess partly out of curiosity he know I very well seem surprise at my condescension and make I compliment upon it as well as he could in the condition he be the people about he say he have be delirious : but when i see he he have he understanding as well as ever i know and speak strong and hearty without any seeming uneasiness or constraint after i have tell he i be sorry to see he in those melancholy circumstance and say some other civility suitable to the occasion i desire he to tell I freely and ingenuously whether the _ prediction _ mr bickerstaff have publish relate to he death have not too much affect and work on he imagination he confess he often have it in he head but never with much apprehension till about a fortnight before : since which time it have the perpetual possession of he mind and thought and he do verily believe be the true natural cause of he present distemper for say he i be thoroughly persuade and i think i have very good reason that mr bickerstaff speak altogether by guess and know no more what will happen this year than i do myself i tell he he discourse surprise I and i would be glad he be in a state of health to be able to tell I what reason he have to be convince of mr bickerstaff 's ignorance he reply i be a poor ignorant fellow breed to a mean trade yet i have sense enough to know that all pretence of foretell by astrology be deceit : for this manifest reason because the wise and learn -lrb- who can only judge whether there be any truth in this science -rrb- do all unanimously agree to laugh at and despise it and none but the poor ignorant vulgar give it any credit and that only upon the word of such silly wretch as i and my fellow who can hardly write or read i then ask he why he have not calculate he own nativity to see whether it agree with bickerstaff 's prediction at which he shake he head and say o sir this be no time for jest but for repent those foolery as i do now from the very bottom of my heart by what i can gather from you say i the observation and prediction you print with you _ almanack _ be mere imposition upon the people he reply if it be otherwise i should have the less to answer for we have a common form for all those thing as to foretell the weather we never meddle with that but leave it to the printer who taketh it out of any old _ almanack _ as he thinketh fit the rest be my own invention to make my _ almanack _ sell have a wife to maintain and no other way to get my bread : for mend old shoe be a poor livelihood and add he sigh i wish i may not have do more mischief by my physic than by astrology although i have some good receipt from my grandmother and my own composition be such as i think could at least do no hurt i have some other discourse with he which now i can not call to mind : and i fear i have already tired you lordship i shall only add one circumstance that on he deathb he declare himself a nonconformist and have a fanatic -lrb- _ the political designation of dissenter _ -rrb- preacher to be he spiritual guide after half a hour 's conversation i take my leave be almost stifle by the closeness of the room i imagine he could not hold out long and therefore withdraw to a little coffee-house hard by leave a servant at the house with order to come immediately and tell I as near as he could the minute when partrige should expire : which be not above two hour after when look upon my watch i find it to be above five minute after seven by which it be clear that mr bickerstaff be mistaken almost four hour in he calculation -lrb- _ see _ p 173 -rrb- in the other circumstance he be exact enough but whether he have not be the cause of this poor man 's death as well as the predictor may be very reasonably dispute however it must be confess the matter be odd enough whether we should endeavor to account for it by chance or the effect of imagination for my own part although i believe no man have less faith in these matter yet i shall wait with some impatience and not without expectation the fulfil of mr bickerstaff 's second prediction that the cardinal de noailles be to die upon the 4th of April -lrb- 1708 -rrb- and if that should be 1708 squire bickerstaff detect or the _ astrological impostor convict _ by john partridge student in physic and astrology -lrb- this be write for partridge either by nichola rowe or dr yalden and put forth by he in good faith in proof of he continued existence -rrb- it be hard my dear countryman of these unite nation it be very hard that a britain bear a protestant astrologer a man of revolution principle a assertor of the liberty and property of the people should cry out in vain for justice against a frenchman a papist and a illiterate pretender to science that would blast my reputation most inhumanly bury I alive and defraud my native country of those service which in my double capacity -lrb- _ physician and astrologer _ -rrb- i daily offer the public what great provocation i have receive let the impartial reader judge and how unwillingly even in my own defense i now enter the list against falsehood ignorance and envy but i be exasperate at length to drag out this cacus from the den of obscurity where he lurketh to detect he by the light of those star he have so impudently traduce and to shew there be not a monster in the sky so pernicious and malevolent to mankind as a ignorant pretender to physic and astrology i shall not directly fall on the many gross error nor expose the notorious absurdity of this prostituted libeller until i have let the learn world fairly into the controversy depend and then leave the unprejudiced to judge of the merit and justice of my cause it be towards the conclusion of the year 1707 -lrb- _ accord to the old way of reckon the year from march 25th the precise date be February 1708 see _ p 469 -rrb- when a impudent pamphlet creep into the world intitule _ prediction & c by isaac bickerstaff esquire _ among the many arrogant assertion lay down by that lie spirit of divination he be please to pitch on the cardinal de noailles and myself among many other eminent and illustrious person that be to die within the confines of the ensue year and peremptorily fix the month day and hour of we death this i think be sport with great man and public spirit to the scandal of religion and reproach of power : and if sovereign prince and astrologer must make diversion for the vulgar why then farewell say i to all government ecclesiastical and civil but i thank my better star i be alive to confront this false and audacious predictor and to make he rue the hour he ever affront a man of science and resentment the cardinal may take what measure he please with he : as he excellency be a foreigner and a papist he have no reason to rely on I for he justification i shall only assure the world that he be alive but as he be breed to letter and be master of a pen let he use it in he own defense in the meantime i shall present the public with a faithful narrative of the ungenerous treatment and hard usage i have receive from the virulent papers and malicious practice of this pretended astrologer a true and impartial account of the proceedings of isaac bickerstaff esq against I the 29th of march _ anno dom _ 1708 be the night this sham prophet have so impudently fix for my last which make little impression on myself but i can not answer for my whole family for my wife with a concern more than usual prevail on I to take somewhat to sweat for a cold and between the hour of 8 and 9 to go to bed the maid as she be warm my bed with the curiosity natural to young woman run to the window and ask of one pass the street who the bell toll for dr partridge say he the famous _ almanack _ maker who die suddenly this evening the poor girl provoke tell he he lie like a rascal the other very sedately reply the sexton have so informed he and if false he be to blame for impose on a stranger she ask a second and a third as they pass and every one be in the same tone now i do not say these be accomplice to a certain astrological squire and that one bickerstaff might be saunter thereabout because i will assert nothing here but what i dare attest and plain matter of fact my wife at this fall into a violent disorder and i must own i be a little discomposed at the oddness of the accident in the meantime one knock at the door betty runneth down and open find a sober grave person who modestly inquire if this be dr partridge 's she take he for some cautious city patient that come at that time for privacy shew he into the dining-room as soon as i could compose myself i go to he and be surprise to find my gentleman mount on a table with a two-foot rule in he hand measure my wall and take the dimension of the room pray sir say i not to interrupt you have you any business with I only sir reply he to order the girl to bring I a better light : for this be but a dim one sir sayeth i my name be partridge oh the doctor 's brother belike cry he the staircase i believe and these two apartment hang in close mourning will be sufficient and only a strip of bay -lrb- cloth -rrb- round the other room the doctor must need die rich he have great dealings in he way for many year if he have no family coat -lrb- of arm -rrb- you have as good use the scutcheon of the company they be as showish and will look as magnificent as if he be descend from the blood-royal with that i assume a greater air of authority and demand who employ he and how he come there why i be send sir by the company of undertaker spectator _ you may remember i tell you before that one cause assign for the lay down the _ tatler _ be want of matter and indeed this be the prevailing opinion in town : when we be surprise all at he : and therefore rather choose to fall on the author and to call out for help to all good christian by assure they again and again that they be the first original true and undisputed _ isaac bickerstaff _ meanwhile the _ spectator _ whom we regard as we shelter from that flood of false wit and impertinence which be break in upon we be in every one 's hand and a constant topic for we morning conversation at tea-table and coffee-house we have at first indeed no manner of notion how a diurnal paper could be continue in the spirit and style of we present _ spectator _ : but to we no small surprise we find they still rise upon we and can only wonder from whence so prodigious a run of wit and learning can proceed since some of we best judge seem to think that they have hitherto in have often think that the conjunction of those two great genius who seem to stand in a class by themselves so high above all we other wit resemble that of two statesman in a late reign whose character addison _ -rrb- be continually at work behind the curtain draw up and prepare all those scheme which the latter still drive on and stand out expose to the world to receive its praise or censure for you own sake in receive compliment of this nature i depend upon you known humanity for pardon when i acknowledge that you have this present trouble for mine when i take myself to be ill treat with upon with the greatest affection and veneration i be sir you most oblige most obedient and most humble servant richard steele edward chamberlayne _ the social position of the english established clergy in 1669 ad _ -lrb- _ anglia notitium _ or the present state of england 1st _ ed _ 1669 -rrb- at present the revenue of the english clergy be generally very small and insufficient : above a third of the best benefice of england have be anciently by the pope 's grant appropriate to monastery be on they dissolution make _ lay fee _ besides what have be take by secret and indirect means through corrupt composition and compact and custom in many other parish and also many estate be wholly exempt from pay tithe as the land that belong to the cistercian monk and to the knight templar and hospitaller and those benefice that be free from these thing be yet -lrb- besides first fruit and tenth to the king and procuration to the bishop -rrb- tax towards the charge of they respective parish and towards the public charge of the nation above and beyond the proportion of the laity the bishopric of england have be also since the latter of henry viii 's reign to the come in of king jame most miserably rob and spoil of the greatest part of they land and revenue so that at this day -lrb- 1669 -rrb- a mean gentleman of # 200 from land yearly will not change he worldly estate and condition with diver bishop : and a attorney a shopkeeper a common artisan will hardly change theirs with the ordinary pastor of the church some few bishopric do yet retain a competency amongst which the bishopric of durham be account one of the chief : the yearly revenue whereof before the late trouble -lrb- _ ie the civil war _ -rrb- be above # 6000 -lrb- = # 25000 _ now _ -rrb- : of which by the late _ act for abolish tenure in capite _ -lrb- 1660 -rrb- be lose about # 2000 yearly out of this revenue a yearly pension of # 800 be pay to the crown ever since the reign of queen elizabeth who promise in lieu thereof so much in impropriation : which be never perform above # 340 yearly be pay to several officer of the county palatine of durham the assizes and session also be duly keep in the bishop 's house at the sole charge of the bishop also the several expense for keep in repair certain bank of river in that bishopric and of several house belong to the bishopric moreover the yearly tenth public tax the charge of go to and wait at parliament be deduct there will remain in ordinary year to the bishop to keep hospitality which must be great and to provide for those of he family but about # 1500 -lrb- = # 4500 _ now _ -rrb- yearly the like might be say of some other principal bishopric the great diminution of the revenue of the clergy and the little care of augment and defend the patrimony of the church be the great reproach and shame of the english reformation and will one day prove the ruin of church and state it be the last trick saith st gregory that the devil have in this world when he can not bring the word and sacrament into disgrace by error and heresy he invent this project to bring the clergy into contempt and low esteem as it be now in england where they be account by many the dross and refuse of the nation man think it a stain to they blood to place they son in that function and woman be ashamed to marry with any of they it have be observe even by stranger that the iniquity of the present time in england be such that the english clergy be not only hate by the romanist on the one side and malign by the presbyterian on the other but also that of all the christian clergy of europe whether romish lutheran or calvinistic none be so little _ respect beloved obey _ or _ reward _ as the present pious learn loyal clergy of england even by those who smoak -lrb- john eachard stp be appoint master of catherine hall cambridge in 1675 -rrb- _ the preface to the reader _ _ i can very easily fancy that many upon the very first sight of the title will presently imagine that the author do either want the great tithe lie under the pressure of some pitiful vicarage or that he be much out of humor and dissatisfied with the present condition of affair or lastly that he write to no purpose at all there have be a abundance of unprofitable adviser in this kind as to my be under some low church dispensation you may know i write not out of a pinch necessity or out of any rise design you may please to believe that although i have a most solemn reverence for the clergy in general and especially for that of england yet for my own part i must confess to you i be not of that holy employment and have as little thought of be dean or bishop as they that think so have hope of be all lord keeper nor less mistaken will they be that shall judge I in the least discontented or any way dispose to disturb the peace of the present settle church : for in good truth i have neither lose king 's nor bishop 's land that should incline I to a surly and quarrelsome complain as many be who would have be glad enough to see he majesty restore and would have endure bishop daintily well have they lose no money by they come in i be not i will assure you any of those occasional writer that miss preferment in the university can presently write you they new way of education or be a little tormented with a ill-chosen wife set forth the doctrine of divorce to be truly evangelical the cause of these few sheet be honest and innocent and as free from all passion as any design as for the last thing which i suppose object _ viz _ that this book be altogether needless there have be a infinite number of church and clergy-mender that have make many tedious and unsuccessful offer : i must need confess that it be very unreasonable for I to expect a better reward only thus much i think with modesty may be say that i can not at present call to mind anything that be propound but what be very hopeful and easily accomplish for indeed should i go about to tell you that a child can never prove a profitable instructor of the people unless bear when the sun be in _ aries _ or bring up in a school that stand full south : that he can never be able ecclesiastical subject and not in relation to the rest it be not sir for you or i to guess which way the whole clergy in general might be better provide for but sure it be and must not be deny that so governor of parish than at present we commonly find there be a way i know that some people love marvellously to talk of and that be a just and equal levelling of ecclesiastical preferment what a delicate refreshment say they would it be if # 20000 or # 30000 a year be take from the bishop and discreetly sprinkle amongst the poorer and meaner sort of the clergy how would it rejoice they heart and encourage they in they office what need those great and sumptuous palace they city and they country house they park and spacious water they costly dish and fashionable sauce may not he that live in a small thatched house that can scarcely walk four stride in he own ground that have only _ read _ well concern venison fish and fowl : may not he i say preach as loud and to as much purpose as one of those high and mighty spiritualist go to then see it have pleased god to make such a bountiful provision for he church in general what need we be solicitous about the emend the low condition of many of the clergy when as there be such a plain remedy at hand have we but grace to apply it this invention please some mainly well but for all the great care they pretend to have of the distressed part of the clergy i be confident one might easily guess what would please they much better if -lrb- instead of augment small benefice -rrb- the bishop would be please to return to they those land purchase in they absence -lrb- _ ie during the commonwealth which be restore to the bishopric at the restoration _ -rrb- : and then as for the relieve of the clergy they would try if they could find out another way but art thou in good earnest my excellent contriver dost thou think that if the greatest of we church preferment be wisely parcel out amongst those that be in want it would do such feat and courtesy and dost thou not likewise think that if ten or twenty of the lustiest nobleman 's estate of england be cleverly slice among the indigent would it not strangely refresh some of the poor laity that cry small coal or grind scissors i do suppose if god should afterwards incline thy mind -lrb- for i fancy it will not be as yet a good while -rrb- to be a benefactor to the church thy wisdom may possibly direct thee to disperse thy goodness in smaller parcel rather than to flow in upon two or three with full happiness but if it be my inclination to settle upon one ecclesiastical person and he successor for ever a # 1000 a year -lrb- = # 3000 _ now _ -rrb- upon condition only to read the _ service _ of the church once in a week and you take it ill and find fault with my prudence and the method of my munificence and say that the stipend be much too large for such a small task : yet i be confident that should i make thy laityship heir of such a estate and oblige thee only to the trouble and expense of spend a single chicken or half a dozen lark once a year in commemoration of I that thou wouldst count I the wisest man that ever be since the creation and pray to god never to dispose thy mind to part with one farthing of it for any other use than for the service of thyself and thy family and yet so it be that because the bishop upon they first be restore -lrb- in 1660 -rrb- have the confidence to levy fine accord as they be justly due and desire to live in they own house if not pull down and to receive they own rent : presently they cry out the churchman have get all the treasure and money of the nation into they hand if they have let they thank god for it and make a good use of it weep not beloved for there be very little hope that they will cast it all into the sea on purpose to stop the mouth of they that say they have too much what other contrivance there may be for the settle upon minister in general a sufficient revenue for they subsistence be not to be avoid but that a great part of they will want learn prudence courage and esteem to do any good where they live and what if we have -lrb- as by all must be acknowledge -rrb- as wise and learn bishop as be in the world and many other of very great understanding and wisdom yet -lrb- as be before hint -rrb- unless there be provide for most town and parish some tolerable and sufficient guide the strength of religion and the credit of the clergy will daily languish more and more not that it be to be believe that every small country parish should be altogether hopeless as to the next life unless they have a hooker a chillingworth a hammond or a sanderson dwelling amongst they : but it be requisite and might be bring about that somebody there should be to whom the people have reason to attend and to be direct and guide by he i have sir no more to say be it not that you find the word _ religion _ in the title : of which in particular i have speak very little neither need i consider how nearly it depend as to its glory and strength upon the reputation and mouth of the priest and i shall add no more but this viz that among those many thing that tend to the decay of religion and of a due reverence of the _ holy scripture _ nothing have more occasion it than the ridiculous and idle discourse that be utter out of pulpit for when the gallant of the world do observe how the minister themselves do jingle quibble and play the fool with the text : no wonder if they who be so inclinable to atheism do not only deride and despise the priest but droll upon the _ bible _ and make a mock of all that be sober and sacred i be sir you most humble servant tb _ August _ 8 1670 finis isaac bickerstaff -lrb- _ ie _ richard steele -rrb- _ the misery of the domestic chaplain in _ 1710 -lrb- _ the tatler _ no 255 Thursday 23 nov 1710 -rrb- _ to the censor of great britain sir i be at present under very great difficulty which be not in the power of any one besides yourself to redress whether or not you shall think it a proper case to come before you court of honor i can not tell : but thus it be i be chaplain to a honorable family very regular at the hour of devotion and i hope of a unblameable life : but for not offer to rise at the second course i find my patron and he lady very sullen and out of humor though at first i do not know the reason of it at length when i happen to help myself to a jelly the lady of the house otherwise a devout woman tell I it do not become a man of my cloth to delight in such frivolous food but as i still continue to sit out the last course i be yesterday inform by the butler that he lordship have no further occasion for my service all which be humbly submit to you consideration by sir you most humble servant & c _ the case of this gentleman deserve pity especially if he love sweetmeat to which if i may guess by he letter he be no enemy in the meantime i have often wonder at the indecency of discard the holiest man from the table as soon as the most delicious part of the entertainment be serve up : and could never conceive a reason for so absurd a custom be it because a licorous palate or a sweet tooth -lrb- as they call it -rrb- be not consistent with the sanctity of he character this be but a trifling pretence no man of the most rigid virtue give offence by any excess in plum pudding or plum porridge and that because they be the first part of the dinner be there anything that tend to _ incitation _ in sweetmeat more than in ordinary dish certainly not sugar-plum be a very innocent diet and conserve of a much colder nature than you common pickle i have sometimes think that the ceremony of the _ chaplain fly away from the dessert _ be typical and figurative to mark out to the company how they ought to retire from all the luscious bait of temptation and deny they appetite the gratification that be most please to they or at least to signify that we ought to stint ourselves in the most lawful satisfaction and not make we pleasure but we support the end of eating but most certainly if such a lesson of temperance have be necessary at a table : we clergy would have recommend it to all the lay master of family and not have disturb other man 's table with such unreasonable example of abstinence the original therefore of this _ barbarous custom _ i take to have be merely accidental the chaplain retire out of pure complaisance to make room for the removal of the dish or possibly for the range of the dessert this by degree grow into a duty till at length as the fashion improve the good man find himself cut off from the third part of the entertainment : and if the arrogance of the patron go on it be not impossible but in the next generation he may see himself reduce to the tithe or tenth dish of the table a sufficient caution not to part with any privilege we be once possess of it be usual for the priest in old time to feast upon the sacrifice nay the honey cake while the hungry laity look upon he with great devotion : or as the late lord rochester describe it in a very lively manner _ and while the priest do eat the people stare _ at present the custom be invert the laity feast while the priest stand by as a humble spectator this necessarily put the good man upon make great ravages on all the dish that stand near he and upon distinguish himself by voraciousness of appetite as know that he time be short i would fain ask these stiff-necked patron whether they would not take it ill of a chaplain that in he grace after meat should return thanks for the whole entertainment with a exception to the dessert and yet i can not but think that in such a proceeding he would but deal with they as they deserve what would a roman catholic priest think -lrb- who be always help first and place next the lady -rrb- should he see a clergyman give he company the slip at the first appearance of the tart or sweetmeat would he not believe that he have the same antipathy to a candid orange or a piece of puff paste as some have to a cheshire cheese or a breast of mutton yet to so ridiculous a height be this foolish custom grow that even the christmas pie which in its very nature be a kind of consecrate cake and a badge of distinction be often forbid to the druid of the family strange that a sirloin of beef whether boil or roasted when entire be expose to he utmost depredation and incision but if mince into small piece and toss up with plum and sugar it change its property and forsooth it be meat for he master in this case i know not which to censure -lrb- _ blame _ -rrb- the patron or the chaplain the insolence of power or the abjectness of dependence for my own part i have often blush to see a gentleman whom i know to have more wit and learning than myself and who be breed up with I at the university upon the same foot of a liberal education treat in such a ignominious manner and sink beneath those of he own rank by reason of that character which ought to bring he honor this deter man of generous mind from place themselves in such a station of life and by that mean frequently exclude person of quality from the improving and agreeable conversation of a learn and obsequious friend mr oldham let we know that he be affright from the thought of such a employment by the scandalous sort of treatment which often accompany it _ some think themselves exalt to the sky if they light in some noble family : diet a horse and thirty pound a year besides th ' advantage of he lordship 's ear the credit of the business and the state be thing that in a youngster 's sense sound _ great _ little the unexperienced wretch do know what slavery he oft must undergo who though in silken scarf and cassock drest wear but a gayer _ livery _ at best when dinner call the implement must wait with holy word to consecrate the meat : but hold it for a favor seldom know if he be deign the honor to sit down soon as the tart appear sir crape withdraw these dainty be not for a spiritual maw observe you distance and be sure to stand hard by the cistern with you cap in hand there for diversion you may pick you tooth till the kind voider come for you relief let other who such meanness can brook strike countenance to every great man 's look : i rate my freedom higher _ the author 's raillery be the raillery of a friend and do not turn the sacred order into ridicule : but it be a just censure on such person as take advantage from the necessity of a man of merit to impose upon he hardship that be by no means suitable to the dignity of he profession nestor ironside -lrb- _ ie richard steele _ -rrb- _ another description of the misery of the domestic chaplain in _ 1713 ad -lrb- _ the guardian _ no 173 Thursday 17 sept 1713 -rrb- when i be dispose to give myself a day 's rest i order the _ lion _ to be open -lrb- _ ie a letter-box at button 's coffee-house _ -rrb- and search into that magazine of intelligence for such letter as be to my purpose the first i look into come to I from one who be chaplain to a great family he treat himself in the beginning of it after such a manner as i be persuade no man of sense would treat he even the lawyer and the physician to a man of quality expect to be use like gentleman and much more may any one of so superior a profession i be by no means encourage that dispute whether the chaplain or the master of the house be the better man and the more to be respect the two learn author dr hick and mr collier -lrb- to whom i might add several other -rrb- be to be excuse if they have carry the point a little too high in favor of the chaplain : since in so corrupt a age as that we live in the popular opinion run so far into the other extreme the only controversy between the patron and the chaplain ought to be which should promote the good design and interest of each other most and for my own part i think it be the happiest circumstance in a great estate or title that it qualify a man for choose out of such a learn and valuable body of man as that of the english clergy a friend a spiritual guide and a companion the letter which i have receive from one of this order be as follow : _ mr guardian i hope you will not only indulge I in the liberty of two or three question but also in the solution of they i haw have the honor many year of be chaplain in a noble family and of be account the _ highest servant _ in the house : either out of respect to my cloth or because i lie in the uppermost garret whilst my old lord live he table be always adorn with useful learning and innocent mirth as well as cover with plenty i be not look upon as a piece of furniture fit only to sanctify and garnish a feast but treat as a gentleman and generally desire to fill up the conversation a hour after i have do my duty _ -lrb- ie say grace after dinner -rrb- _ but now my young lord be come to the estate i find i be look upon as a _ censor morum _ a obstacle to mirth and talk : and suffer to retire constantly with _ prosperity to the church _ in my mouth _ -lrb- ie after drink this toast -rrb- _ i declare solemnly sir that i have hear nothing from all the fine gentleman who visit we more remarkable for half a year than that one young lord be seven time drunk at genoa i have lately take the liberty to stay three or four round _ -lrb- ie of the bottle -rrb- _ beyond -lrb- the toast of -rrb- _ the church _ to see what topic of discourse they go upon : but to my great surprise have hardly hear a word all the time besides the toast then they all stare full in my face and shew all the action of uneasiness till i be go immediately upon my departure to use the word of a old comedy i find by the noise they make that they have a mind to be private i be at a loss to imagine what conversation they have among one another which i may not be present at : since i love innocent mirth as much as any of they and be shock with no freedom whatsoever which be inconsistent with christianity i have with much ado maintain my post hitherto at the dessert and every day eat a tart in the face of my patron : but how long i shall be invest with this privilege i do not know for the servant who do not see I support as i be in my old lord 's time begin to brush very familiarly by I : and they thrust aside my chair when they set the sweetmeat on the table i have be service which you useful papers daily do to religion this perhaps will not be the least : and it will lay a very great obligation on you unknown servant gw _ benjamin franklin _ poor richard improve must have be gratify by a incident i be go to relate to you i stop my horse lately where a great number of people be collect at a vendue -lrb- _ sale _ -rrb- of merchant 's goods the hour of sale not be come they be converse on the badness of the time : and one of the company call to a clean old man with white lock pray father abraham what do you think of the time will not these heavy tax quite enough _ and _ many word will not fill a bushel _ as _ poor richard _ say they all join desire he to speak he mind and gathering round he he proceed as follow : friend say he and neighbor the tax be indeed very heavy and if those lay on by the government be the only one we have to pay we might the more easily discharge they : but we have many other and much more grievous to some of we we be tax twice as much by we idleness three time as much by we pride and four time as much by we folly : and from these tax the commissioner can not ease or deliver we by allow a abatement however let we hearken to good advice and something may be do for we _ god help they that help themselves _ as _ poor richard _ say in he _ almanac _ of 1733 it would be think a hard government that should tax its people one-tenth part of they time to be employ in its service but idleness tax many of we much more if we reckon all that be spend in absolute sloth or do of nothing with that which be spend in idle employment or amusement that amount to nothing sloth by bring on disease absolutely shorten life _ sloth like rust consume faster than labor wear while the use key be always bright as poor richard say _ but _ dost thou love life then do not squander time for that be the stuff life be make of _ as _ poor richard say _ how much more than be necessary do we spend in sleep forget that _ the sleep fox catch no poultry _ and that _ there will be sleep enough in the grave _ as _ poor richard _ say if time be of all thing the most precious _ wasting of time must be _ -lrb- as _ poor richard _ say -rrb- _ the greatest prodigality _ since as he elsewhere tell we _ lose time be never find again _ and what we call _ time enough always prow little enough _ let we then up and be do and do to the purpose : so by diligence shall we do more with less perplexity _ sloth make all thing difficult but industry all thing easy _ as _ poor richard _ say : and _ he that riseth late must trot all day and shall scarce overtake he business at night _ while _ laziness travel so slowly that poverty soon overtake he _ as we read in _ poor richard _ who add _ drive thy business let not that drive thee _ and _ early to bed and early to rise make a man healthy wealthy and wise _ so what signify _ wish _ and _ hope _ for better time we may make these time better if we bestir ourselves _ industry need not wish _ as _ poor richard _ say and _ he that live on hope will die fast there be no gain without pain _ then _ help hand for i have no land _ or if i have they be smartly tax and as _ poor richard _ likewise observe _ he that have a trade have a estate _ and he that _ have a call have a office of profit and honor _ : but then the trade must be work at and the call well follow or neither the estate nor the office will enable we to pay we tax if we be industrious we shall never starve for as _ poor richard _ say _ at the work man 's house hunger look in but dare not enter _ nor will the bailiff or the constable enter : for _ industry pay debt while despair increaseth they _ say _ poor richard _ what though you have find no treasure nor have any rich relation leave you a legacy _ diligence be the mother of good-luck _ as _ poor richard _ say and _ god give ail thing to industry _ then _ plough deep while sluggard sleep and you shall have corn to sell and to keep _ say _ poor dick _ work while it be call to-day for you know not how much you may be hinder to-morrow : which make _ poor richard _ say _ one to-day be worth two to-morrow _ and farther _ have you somewhat to do to-morrow do it to-day _ if you be a servant would you not be ashamed that a good master should catch you idle be you then you own master _ be ashamed to catch yourself idle _ as _ poor dick _ say when there be so much to be do for yourself you family you country and you gracious king be up by peep of day _ let not the sun look down and say inglorious here he lie _ handle you tool without mitten remember that _ the cat in glove catch no mouse _ as _ poor richard _ say ` tis true there be much to be do and perhaps you be weak handed but stick to it steadily and you will see great effect for _ constant drop wear away stone _ and _ by diligence and patience the mouse eat in two the cable _ and _ little stroke fall great oak _ as _ poor richard _ say in he _ almanac _ the year i can not just now remember methink i hear some of you say must a man afford himself no leisure i will tell thee my friend what _ poor richard _ say _ employ thy time well if thou meanest to gain leisure and life of leisure and a life of laziness be two thing _ do you imagine that sloth will afford you more comfort than labor no for as _ poor richard _ say _ trouble spring from idleness and grievous toil into debt _ : and again to the same purpose _ lie ride upon debt 's back _ whereas a free bear englishman ought not to be ashamed or afraid to see or speak to any man living but poverty often deprive a man of all spirit and virtue _ ` tis hard for a empty bag to stand upright _ as _ poor richard _ truly say what would you think of that prince or the government who should issue a edict forbid you privilege and such a government tyrannical and yet you be about to put yourself under that tyranny when you run in debt for such dress you creditor have authority at he pleasure to deprive you of you poor richard _ tell we -rrb- _ have better memory than debtor _ and in another place say _ creditor be a superstitious sect great observer of set day and time _ the day come round before you be aware and the demand be make before you be prepare to satisfy it : or if you bear you debt in mind the term which at first seem so long will as it lessen appear extremely short time will seem to have add wing to he heel as well as shoulder _ those have a short lend _ saith _ poor richard who owe money to be pay at easter _ then since as he say _ the borrower be a slave to the lender and the debtor to the creditor _ disdain the chain preserve you freedom and maintain you independency be industrious and free be frugal and free at present perhaps you may think yourself in thrive circumstance and that you can bear a little extravagance without injury : but _ for age and want save while you may no morning sun last a whole day _ as _ poor richard _ say gain may be temporary and uncertain but ever while you live expense be constant and certain : and _ ` tis easier to build two chimney than to keep one in fuel _ as _ poor richard _ say so _ rather go to bed supperless than rise in debt _ _ get what you can and what you get hold ` tis the stone that will turn all you lead into gold _ as _ poor richard _ say and when you have get the philosopher 's stone sure you will no longer complain of bad time or the difficulty of pay tax this doctrine my friend be reason and wisdom but after all do not depend too much upon you own industry and frugality and prudence though excellent thing for they and be afterwards prosperous and now to conclude _ experience keep a dear school but fool will learn in no other and scarce in that _ for it be true _ we may give advice but we can not give conduct _ as _ poor richard _ say however remember this _ they that will not be counsel can not be help _ as _ poor richard _ say : and farther that _ if you will not hear reason she 'll surely rap you knuckle _ thus the old gentleman end he harangue the people hear it and approve the doctrine and immediately practice the contrary just as if it have be a common sermon for the vendue open and they begin to buy extravagantly notwithstanding all he caution and they own fear of tax i find the good man have thoroughly study my _ almanac _ and digest all i have drop on those topic during the course of five and twenty year the frequent mention he make of I must have tire any one else but my vanity be wonderfully delighted with it : though i be conscious that not a tenth part of the wisdom be my own which he ascribe to I but rather the gleanings i have make of the sense of all age and nation however i resolve to be the better for the echo of it and though i have at first determine to buy stuff for a new coat i go away resolve to wear my old one a little longer reader if thou wilt do the same thy profit will be as great as mine i be as ever thine to serve thee July 7 1757 richard saunder end of
